Property -- Open House: SoHo Adds to Reputation for 'Lofty' Sales Prices
By Gabby Warshawer
1173 words
22 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

For the past several years SoHo's residential real-estate market has been among the most expensive in New York City, a trend that is expected to continue given the extremely limited amount of new development in the Lower Manhattan neighborhood.

After the former manufacturing hub was colonized by artists who lived and worked in its lofts in the 1970s, the spaces became coveted residential real estate in subsequent decades.

In addition, the historic-preservation movement in SoHo, which happened in tandem with its revival as a residential area, sought to protect its many cast-iron buildings in the face of a Robert Moses plan to construct two elevated highways in the neighborhood. Most of SoHo was given landmark designation in 1973.

The area has also become more of a tourist destination over the past couple of decades, and shoppers flock to Broadway's stores.

While record sales prices were reached in other Manhattan neighborhoods, median real-estate prices for SoHo residential properties were the second highest in the city last year, trailing only neighboring TriBeCa, according to data compiled by the real-estate website StreetEasy.com. The overall median sales price in SoHo was $2.5 million in 2013, compared with $2.6 million in TriBeCa.

For new developments, StreetEasy said the median price in SoHo last year was $3.9 million compared with $2.9 million paid in TriBeCa. There were only 23 new condo sales during the year in SoHo, compared with TriBeCa's 127.

Since most of SoHo falls within a historic district, there are significant constraints on new construction, which contributes to a smaller pool of real-estate inventory.

"You're looking at a very unique housing stock in SoHo that is very difficult to get anywhere else," says StreetEasy's Alan Lightfeldt. "The full-floor lofts are extremely limited in supply and highly desirable."

There are only a few new condominiums currently under construction in the neighborhood, including One Vandam, a 25-unit limestone-and-glass building that is being constructed on Sixth Avenue and Spring Street.

The building's developer said the limited number and exclusive nature of new developments in SoHo help to account for the higher price tags in the neighborhood.

"Most of the new projects are of a boutique scale, which allows a developer to take more design risks, adding to the special quality of the buildings there," said Timothy Quinlan, a principal at New York-based Quinlan Development Group, which is developing One Vandam in partnership with Tavros Development Partners.

Units in One Vandam are priced around $3,000 a foot, and the most expensive penthouse, a triplex, has an asking price of $28 million.

While the building is only expected to begin rising out of the ground in the coming days, more than 40% of the units are now in contract, according to Mr. Quinlan, who said One Vandam is on pace to be completed in the early spring of next year.

Residents and real-estate brokers say that the area continues to grow as an international destination both for apartment buyers and tourists.

A business improvement district covering Broadway from Houston to Canal streets was approved in October after being in the planning stages for five years.

A major impetus for the district's creation was the high volume of tourist and shopper foot traffic on the thoroughfare.

"It's about managing the success of Broadway because the retailers and tourists result in such large crowds," said Barbara Cohen, a consultant to the district's formation.

Ms. Cohen said the district is intended to supplement city services such as sanitation that are strained by Broadway's high volume of pedestrian traffic.

Parks: Park space isn't in abundance in SoHo. Vesuvio Playground, on Thompson and Spring streets, has basketball and handball courts, as well as a small pool.

Schools: There are no public schools in the neighborhood because the zoning for manufacturing uses doesn't allow them. Schools in nearby districts serving the area include P.S. 130 in Little Italy, an elementary school that received an A grade from the city on its most recent progress report. Elisabeth Irwin High School, on Charlton Street, is the upper school for the West Village's Little Red School House, an independent school founded in the early 1920s.

Dining: SoHo's array of dining options runs the gamut from cheap and casual to trendy and pricey. Balthazar Restaurant, a tourist favorite, serves French bistro staples such as steak frites and duck confit. Isola Trattoria & Crudo Bar, located in the Mondrian SoHo Hotel, specializes in the cuisine of the Amalfi Coast with entrees that include monkfish and fettuccine with lobster.

Entertainment: The many contemporary art galleries in SoHo include Opera Gallery on Spring Street and AFA on Greene Street. The Angelika Film Center on West Houston Street screens independent films.

Shopping: There is no want for shopping options in the neighborhood, which is home to the many clothing retailers lining Broadway -- among them Victoria's Secret, Old Navy, Uniqlo and Bloomingdale's -- as well as smaller designer boutiques on side streets, such as 3.1 Phillip Lim and Marc Jacobs.

Home Sales Fall to 18-Month Low Amid Low Inventories
Conor Dougherty
858 words
21 February 2014
14:05
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Home sales fell in January to their lowest level in 18 months as higher prices and mortgage rates squeezed buyers who continue to face shortages of properties for sale.

Sales of previously owned homes fell by 5.1% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.62 million, the National Association of Realtors said Friday. The median sales price in January stood at $188,900, up 10.7% from a year earlier.

Friday's report shows how the housing market is struggling to shift gears, from a marketplace propelled by investors chasing foreclosed properties to one where first-time and trade-up buyers play a larger role now that the supply of distressed properties has dropped sharply.

Sales volumes are being challenged by reduced affordability of homes and severe winter weather in many parts of the country—two factors putting a damper on demand.

But real-estate agents say the market also is being constrained by a lack of supply. There were only 1.9 million homes on the market in January, according to the Realtors' group. While that was a 2.2% increase from December, only three months during the past 12 years have seen inventories at even lower levels.

Whether the market picks up in the spring depends on whether homeowners like Ravi Parikh, an information-technology consultant in the Chicago area, puts his home on the block.

Mr. Parikh, 51 years old, owns his Hoffman Estates, Ill., home outright, and is looking to trade up to something bigger. But so far he hasn't listed, as he can't find anything good to buy. "I am finding a limited number of houses in [my] price range. If something comes up in that prices range it gets snapped up immediately," he said.

Construction of new homes also remains near its lowest level in 50 years. Builders completed fewer than 570,000 new single-family homes last year, down from an average of 1.1 million from 1990 through 2003.

Economists worry that if inventory levels don't improve, the housing recovery faces a rough road, as the boost from distressed-property investors and ultralow interest rates—which sparked the rebound over the past two years—weakens.

The lack of inventory is "greatly hindering the buyers' enthusiasm," says Lawrence Yun, chief economist at the National Association of Realtors. "They're making a major expenditure. They don't want to see two homes—they want to see 10 homes before buying."

Two years ago, sellers' primary concern was selling their home for less than they wanted—or, in many cases, for less than they owed on their mortgage. Today, homeowners worry they will sell their homes before they can find something that they can afford to buy.

Take Amy Lass, a 32-year-old lawyer in Carlsbad, Calif. Ms. Lass and her husband bought their current home, a $400,000 townhouse, near the bottom of the market. It is now worth around $525,000.

After reaching out to a real-estate agent, Ms. Lass says she isn't sure she will list her house any time soon. That is because prices in the San Diego area have risen so fast—and inventory has fallen so low—she is worried about finding another place to live. Just as the rising price of her home has given her access to a six-figure down payment, four-bedroom homes in her area have risen to around $750,000 to $800,000—out of her price range.

"It's a double-edged sword. I'm going to reap the benefit when I sell, but on the flip side I don't have as much buying power," she said. "It's not like I can sell high and buy low."

Economists expect inventory to rise in the spring, as higher prices entice more sellers to list. Rising home prices have also created a new pool of potential sellers who until recently were "underwater"—owning homes worth less than their mortgages.

While the inventory problem can be "self-perpetuating, sooner or later, when there's not enough inventory, prices rise and that draws people to sell," said Glenn Kelman, chief executive of Redfin, a real-estate brokerage.

Most housing markets in the country are experiencing low inventory, but there are some exceptions. Several markets that witnessed the biggest price rebounds over the past year, including Atlanta, Phoenix, Miami, Sacramento, Calif., and Orlando, Fla., saw inventories post double-digit increases at the end of 2013 from a year earlier, according to The Wall Street Journal's quarterly survey of market conditions in 28 major metro areas.

But even in places where inventories are increasing, "the level is still much lower than it was two, three four year ago," says Svenja Gudell, director of economic research at Zillow Inc., the real-estate information company. "It's hard to sell homes when you don't have any to sell."

A report Thursday from Zillow showed that national home values increased 0.2% in January from a month earlier, the smallest rise since May 2012.

U.S. Existing-Home Sales Fell in January; Drop Pushes Sales Down to Lowest Level in 18 Months
Josh Mitchell
579 words
21 February 2014
06:37
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTON—A broad measure of home sales fell sharply in January, the latest sign the housing market is buckling under higher mortgage rates, tight inventory and severely cold weather.

Sales of previously owned homes fell 5.1% in January from December to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.62 million, the National Association of Realtors said Friday. The drop, the fifth in the past six months, pushed sales down to the lowest level in 18 months.

The decline was steeper than economists' expectations and raises new questions about the underlying strength of the housing market. Sales began slowing in the middle of last year, long before chilly winter and snowstorms gripped much of the U.S.

Existing-home sales, which reflect more than 90% of all home purchases, fell across all four regions in January, but the biggest drop occurred in the West, which has been the warmest.

"January existing home sales left much to be desired, but don't blame it on the weather," said Bill Banfield, vice president of mortgage lender Quicken Loans. "Low home inventories in various parts of the country don't give buyers as many options as they would like right now, causing some to wait until their dream home comes along."

Lawrence Yun, chief economist of the National Association of Realtors, said other factors, along with the cold, are at play, including higher mortgage rates and prices, a shortage of properties for sale, and tight lending standards.

In some places, such as the West, a run-up in prices has prompted many prospective buyers to step back and rethink buying, he said.

Tight inventory is also crimping sales, particularly among lower-priced properties, he said. "The lack of supply I believe is greatly hindering the buyers' enthusiasm," Mr. Yun said. "They're making a major expenditure. They don't want to see two homes—they want to see 10 homes before buying."

Other economists said they believe fundamentals of the housing market remain strong and that sales will pick up as the weather warms.

Friday's report showed existing-home sales were down 5.1% from a year earlier.

The median sales price of existing homes in January was $188,900, up 10.7% from a year earlier. Inventory remains tight by historical standards but is growing. At the January sales pace, it would take 4.9 months to exhaust the supply of homes on the market, up from 4.6 months in December.

The average rate on a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage edged up this week to 4.33%, nearly a percentage point higher from May, according to Freddie Mac.

Rates are projected to rise further as the Federal Reserve moves to unwind its bond-buying program by year-end. The Fed in recent months has moved to cut its monthly bond purchases to $65 billion from $85 billion. Officials have signaled they would make further cuts in coming months if the economy improves as they expect.

Other data suggest the housing market has cooled. Home-builder confidence fell sharply this month, the National Association of Home Builders reported this week. Housing starts—a measure of new construction—fell by 16% in January from December, the Commerce Department reported.

New-home sales dropped in December to the lowest level since the summer, according to an earlier Commerce Department report.

Looking ahead, the attention will turn Saturday to the meeting of finance ministers and central bankers from the Group of 20 industrial and developing nations in Australia. English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

SINGAPORE—Singapore plans to keep property-market cooling measures in place for the time being, its finance minister said Friday, even as real-estate prices show signs of stabilizing after large increases in recent years.

The government, meanwhile, plans to increase social spending to help elderly Singaporeans cope with health-care and living costs, as well as extend efforts to boost efficiency in local companies, Deputy Prime Minister and Finance Minister Tharman Shanmugaratnam told Parliament in his annual budget speech.

These measures are part of a long-term plan to improve economic productivity and cut dependence on foreign workers, whom many citizens blame for overcrowding, soaring home prices and depressed low-end pay.

"Given the run-up in prices in the last four years, it is too early to start relaxing our measures," Mr. Tharman said. "The government will continue to monitor the property market in the coming quarters and adjust our measures when necessary."

His comments came after government data last month showed private-home prices fell 0.9% in the fourth quarter from the third, snapping six straight quarters of growth, as government interventions crimped real-estate investment. Property analysts say prices could fall further this year as large supplies of new homes reach the market amid a rise in interest rates.

Singapore has imposed a series of property-market curbs since September 2009 to combat upward price pressures from low interest rates and abundant capital inflows. The latest measures-tighter property-loan rules introduced in June-have hurt demand, though prices are still up 61% since the end of June 2009.

In a wide-ranging speech, Mr. Tharman said the government expects to spend more than S$9 billion on health-care and other benefits to about 450,000 elderly citizens currently aged 65 and older. This would be funded by money from a new S$8 billion fund, which about half would be spent over the next decade, he said.

More money will be spent to help businesses become more efficient and support their overseas expansion, while employers will be required to contribute more to workers' health-care pensions, the minister said.

The government will also take steps to curb the construction sector's reliance on low-skilled migrant labor, imposing higher levies on the industry's hiring of "basic-skilled" foreigners from July 2016, Mr. Tharman said, adding that foreign-workforce growth is expected to keep slowing over the next two years.

"Singaporean businesses likely heaved a sigh of relief that there was no further tightening of the foreign manpower policies for now, with the exception of the construction sector," Oversea-Chinese Banking Corp., Singapore's second-largest lender by assets, said in a note.

In tax changes announced Friday, the government increased duties on tobacco and alcohol effective Friday, while higher levies in lottery betting will kick in from July.

For the fiscal year starting April 1, the government expects to post an overall deficit of S$1.16 billion-its first budget deficit since fiscal year 2009. Even so, this deficit won't be funded using past reserves as Singapore has accumulated sufficient surpluses in recent years, Mr. Tharman said.

Expenditure for the coming fiscal year is estimated at S$56.66 billion, with another S$3.61 billion to be spent on social, economic and wage-supplement programs, compared with an operating revenue of S$59.51 billion, according to a government statement.

For the current fiscal year, Singapore expects a budget surplus of S$3.92 billion, compared with the S$2.4 billion originally estimated, due to lower-than-expected public-infrastructure spending and higher revenues from vehicle levies, among other factors.

U.S. Chill Presents a Quandary
By Alexandra Scaggs and Dan Strumpf
685 words
21 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
18
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

"Can we stop talking about the weather?"

That cry from Federated Investors analysts in their weekly email to clients Tuesday echoed the feeling of many weary traders and investors over the past few weeks.

But the brutal freeze affecting much of the U.S. presents more than just fodder for idle small talk in financial markets these days. It also presents an unexpected conundrum for many investors: Will the subzero wind chills and snowstorms have a lasting effect on the economy? And if so, which stocks should investors buy and sell?

"The amount of conversations with clients about this weather theme has reached a fever pitch," said Joseph Spinelli, who heads cash equity trading for the Americas at Deutsche Bank's New York office.

Mr. Spinelli has been in regular contact with the bank's own energy analysts -- who are plugged into the weather because of its impact on commodity prices -- for insights into historical weather patterns and their impact on certain sectors. "It's the topic du jour," he said.

The weather has been blamed for two weak jobs reports in a row as well as poor retail sales, construction and manufacturing data. With plenty of winter left, the weather could be a factor to contend with for weeks, or even months, before any distortions are reversed.

For now, many investors take the view that those weak economic trends will reverse when the thaw comes. But for others, the cold snap has been a reason to change gears in portfolios that had been betting on stronger economic growth.

Huntington Asset Advisors Inc. has been cutting back exposure to subsectors like auto makers, glass makers and home builders because of the weather, said Randy Bateman, chief investment officer. Huntington has been selling shares of those stocks or hedging against declines with options strategies, he said.

"The weather is going to affect auto sales, retail sales, employment . . . it's certainly going to affect the housing area," said Mr. Bateman, whose firm manages $14.5 billion in Columbus, Ohio.

One result of the bad weather has been the kind of blizzard seen only on Wall Street: research reports. Deutsche Bank this week published a report titled, "Winter's Worst Puts Pessimists in Full Bloom." BNP Paribas currency strategists joined in on Tuesday, saying the U.S. dollar outlook "remains clouded by weather."

Morgan Stanley's economic group last week tried to estimate what economic growth would look like were it not for the bad weather.

"The geographic and intramonth granularity in our primary datasets suggest a meaningful drag from weather in many key economic series," they wrote. The firm's "counterfactual" estimates included pending home sales rising 7.8% in January instead of 0.2% and retail sales gaining 0.5% excluding gasoline and auto sales, instead of declining 0.2%.

The weather has loomed large for investors because many started 2014 with high economic expectations. Many tilted their portfolios toward stocks that stood to benefit from a continued upswing in growth. Fed officials, too, expressed optimism on the economy as they have started to cut stimulus efforts.

That means a lot rides on whether the weather really is behind the U.S. economy's chill.

Eric Stein, co-director of the $13 billion global income group at Eaton Vance Corp., said for now, he and his team in Boston are treating the weather as "noise." He said some of the obsession with the weather stems simply from the financial-services sector's being concentrated in hard-hit areas like New York and other parts of the Northeast.

"To some extent, the people who are in the financial business kind of overextrapolate what's happening to them to everyone else," he said.

Eric Marshall, portfolio manager with the $1.8 billion Hodges Funds, decided to try and take advantage of that noise. He bought shares of cement and concrete maker Eagle Materials Inc. as shares slid after the company said quarterly earnings and sales missed analysts' forecasts.

MANSION --- Spread Sheet: In Tough Times, the Fix Is In --- When the economy weakens and home prices drop, many homeowners boost spending on routine repairs and maintenance, a study shows
By Sanette Tanaka
639 words
21 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M6
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

When the economy is down, many homeowners look for places to scrimp and save. Home maintenance isn't one of them.

Rather than cut back, homeowners increase the amount they spend on routine repairs and maintenance, such as painting, during poor housing cycles, says Tammy Leonard, clinical assistant professor of economics at the University of Texas at Dallas, who studied maintenance expenditures of households between 2001 and 2009.

Between 2007 and 2009, after the housing bubble burst in 2006, homeowners upped their maintenance spending by an annual average of 63%, or $423 for the average household, compared with an 18% annual average increase in the period between 2001 and 2007 when house prices were rising. When combining all sample years, the average household spent $671, adjusted to year-2000 dollars, annually on maintenance.

Homeowners may have decided to spend more to maintain their homes because they expected to stay there longer.

"Their expectations in the tenure of their house may have changed," Prof. Leonard says. "No one wanted to sell in 2007, and people have thought they would invest."

The study's findings applied to all housing groups except for one -- households with low home equity and low income. That pool of homeowners likely faced a high risk of default and slashed their maintenance costs to nil when the market turned.

Prof. Leonard examined roughly 10,000 to 12,000 single-family homes, based on data from the American Housing Survey administered by the U.S. Census. Households reported their maintenance costs as well as the estimated value of their home, which determined their loan-to-value ratio as well as their perceived default risk.

The study, "The Impact of Housing Market Conditions on Residential Property Upkeep," was published in Housing Studies in January 2013.

Madeleine Romanello, broker associate with One Sotheby's International Realty in Miami Beach, Fla., says homeowners may increase their spending in poor housing cycles to help their property sell.

"[Good maintenance] is a major, major factor in selling a listing," Ms. Romanello says. "The house has to look completely pristine. Every light bulb needs to be working; the house has to be totally clean."

Some buyers believe cosmetic issues indicate larger functional or structural problems. "The minute buyers see any sort of neglect that's visible, they think there are more underlying problems with the property," she adds.

"In the buyer's mind, one little thing can trigger a negative feeling about the whole area," says Linda Olson, an agent with Better Homes and Gardens Real Estate David Winans & Associates in Arlington, Texas.

Four weeks ago, Ms. Olson got a $182,000 listing for a two-story, well-maintained home in Mansfield, Texas. Within a week, five agents had criticized the neighborhood. One agent mentioned a nearby house, which had overgrown grass, chipped paint and an old truck in the driveway.

Ms. Olson promptly knocked on the neighbor's door. "I said, we were really trying to sell the house, and it would help me out a lot if you could cut the grass and rake the leaves and move your truck," she says. Fortunately, the neighbor obliged.

"I'm negotiating an offer right now," Ms. Olson adds.

---

Fit and Finish

House prices generally rose from 2001 to 2007 and fell from 2007 to 2009. During the latter period, homeowners increased the amount they spent on maintaining their home.

Percentage by which households increased annual maintenance costs, on average:

-- 18%, or $120, 2001 to 2007

-- 63%, or $423, 2007 to 2009

Note: Adjusted to year-2000 dollars

Source: Housing Studies, January 2013

Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Five years after the end of the U.S. recession, the number of Americans who are behind on their mortgages and the backlog of homes in the foreclosure process are finally narrowing to prerecession levels.

The U.S. mortgage delinquency rate—loans that are a payment or more behind but not yet in foreclosure—fell to 6.39% of loans in the fourth quarter of 2013, down from 7.09% a year ago and the lowest rate since the early months of recession in the first quarter of 2008, according to a report Thursday by the Mortgage Bankers Association.

The backlog of foreclosure inventory also fell to its lowest level since 2008, while the number of loans on which lenders initiated foreclosure was the lowest since 2006, which was when the housing bubble was starting to burst.

The report on foreclosures and delinquencies comes as news in the housing-sales market points to a cooling after a sharp run-up in sales and prices during 2013. In the fourth quarter, a combination of rising prices and higher interest rates eroded housing affordability and pushed many buyers to the sidelines.

But the decline in troubled loans means that even if the cooling trend continues or intensifies, there is less danger of the market getting clobbered by a massive foreclosure wave.

Another encouraging sign: Three-quarters of the nation's troubled loans were made in 2007 or earlier, and delinquency rates for loans made after that point are around historical norms, according to the Mortgage Bankers Association. "The legacy of very high foreclosure rates is a problem of older loans," said Michael Fratantoni, the MBA's chief economist.

Foreclosures are down partly because the economy and unemployment rate have improved. Also, banks have reined in many of the looser lending practices that allowed many borrowers to get in over their head.

The boost in home prices, which began about two years ago in many parts of the country, also helped. Overall prices in December were 8.4% higher than one year earlier, according to Black Knight Financial Services, formerly LPS.

This rise has rescued many of the "underwater" borrowers whose home values fell so far during the recession that they owed more on their mortgage than the home was worth. In October, 11.4% of loans were "underwater," according to Black Knight, down from about 19% at the start of last year.

The improvement in foreclosures has been uneven. Just as some regions have boomed and others have lagged behind in the economic recovery, so has it been with the hangover of foreclosures left on the market. The strength of regional economies, state-to-state foreclosure processes and varying responses to the housing bust have led to different rates of healing.

Western markets like California and Arizona were among the hardest hit by the real-estate bust, but now have foreclosure inventories that rank among the bottom handful of states.

Take Brentwood, Calif., a suburb on the edge of the San Francisco Bay Area. At the nadir of the housing bust in 2009, a majority of homes for sale in the area involved foreclosures, said Casey McCann, Brentwood's community development director. "The entire city was affected," he said.

But, like many U.S. cities, investors descended on Brentwood to buy homes for cash that they rented out or flipped to new owners. Mr. McCann says today the share of foreclosures for sale is around 17%. Some 1.25% of California mortgages were in foreclosure at the end of 2013, well below the national average, according to the MBA.

Some states are further along than others in reducing the backlog of foreclosures. Most of the nation's highest foreclosure inventory rates are in the "judicial states," where banks must get court approval to foreclose.

Florida, a judicial state, had the nation's highest share of loans in foreclosure, 8.56%. Still, that was down to a bit more than half of its peak rate. New Jersey and New York—also judicial states—were next on the list and were the only other states with foreclosure inventory rates above 6%. The MBA said that 15 of the 17 states where the foreclosure inventory was higher than the national average were judicial states.

Judicial review has stretched out the foreclosure process, in part because banks have struggled to provide the proper paperwork to demonstrate ownership of mortgages. That has given homeowners more time to work out their debts. But some economists say it has hindered housing markets by slowing the repossession of abandoned or blighted properties.

REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The BOE's Forward Confusion
665 words
20 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
9
English
Copyright © 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Mark Carney has been governor of the Bank of England for fewer than eight months and he has already sacrificed his push for "forward guidance" -- an attempt to make monetary policy more transparent and predictable -- on the altar of easy money. Last August, he and the British central bank's Monetary Policy Committee targeted 7% unemployment as the point at which they would start to raise interest rates. The jobless rate has since fallen more quickly than expected and is now set to pass that mark in March. Instead of sticking to the plan, the BOE last week decided to keep interest rates near zero at least through 2015.

Last year's caveats leaving the door open for early rate rises have given way to vague conditions that must be met before they could conceivably rise. For instance, Mr. Carney reassured markets and businesses that the money faucets will stay open until there is a "business recovery." This allowed him to claim that forward guidance is not dead but merely going into "phase two."

While that sounds like a bad Monty Python script, to be fair Mr. Carney's capitulation follows a similar flip-flop at the U.S. Federal Reserve. The Fed set a 7% unemployment rate as its forward-guidance target for ending bond buying and 6.5% as the trigger for raising rates. The American jobless rate is now 6.6% and the bond buying continues at $65 billion per month, tapered from $85 billion.

The way the markets fell out of bed last May and June, when former Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke mooted the taper, was enough to spook the Fed. That evidently convinced him that the U.S. economy needed more time under his free-money treatment to regain full health. If trillions of dollars in stock-market value were wiped out in a selloff, he feared the reverse wealth effect could harm the recovery.

Mr. Carney came to the same conclusion, despite the BOE's projection of 3.4% GDP growth this year. The U.K. housing market is recovering, and in London there are already fears of a new bubble, with real estate prices rising at a 10% annual clip. So while consumer prices remain stable -- 1.9% at an annual rate in January and there's no sign of deflation -- asset prices are signaling potential trouble.

That's consistent with how easy money can flow through the economy. In the early and mid-2000s, it flowed into housing and commodities. In the current round it is going into property and stocks; the FTSE 100 and S&P 500 indices rose 14% and 30% last year respectively. In the search for yield, investors also move into more speculative plays. The longer the cost of capital remains artificially low on both sides of the Atlantic, the greater the risk of malinvestment and new debt workouts once the liquidity is withdrawn.

The lesson to be drawn from the failure of forward guidance is that policy makers invest too much faith in the power of monetary policy to revive economies. Loose money may be indispensable during and right after a panic to offset the collapse in the velocity of money (how often it changes hands) and restore market stability. But central banks can be left pushing on a string if governments don't undertake reforms to spur new business investment and hiring.

It should be obvious but seems to require saying: Forward guidance can only reduce market uncertainty if central bankers set targets and stick to them. Their ultimate goal should be price stability, not a particular level of employment. Underwriting stock and real estate prices is even dicier given the difficulty of knowing when to stop.

It's past time the BOE and Federal Reserve returned monetary policy to normality. The onus for making reforms to revive economies belongs on elected politicians.

U.S. News: Housing Starts Tumble as Winter Muddles Outlook
By Ben Leubsdorf
675 words
20 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
6
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- Construction of new homes tumbled in January, the latest sign of cooling in the housing market as much of the U.S. shivered through a cold and snowy winter.

U.S. housing starts fell 16% last month to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 880,000, the lowest level since September, the Commerce Department said Wednesday.

That was down from an upwardly revised December rate of 1.05 million new homes built, marking the largest month-over-month decline since February 2011. Starts on single-family homes sank 15.9% in January to an annual pace of 573,000.

Building permits, a sign of future construction, fell 5.4% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 937,000 last month from December's upwardly revised rate of 991,000.

Economists surveyed by Dow Jones had expected smaller declines in both measures, predicting housing starts would fall 4.9% to 950,000 and building permits would fall 1.1% to 980,000.

Winter weather likely dragged down the housing sector in January after strong growth throughout most of 2013. Many economists already have blamed cold and snow in the eastern U.S. for a weaker-than-expected performance by retailers and factories this winter.

"In short, this data tells us nothing about the underlying state of the housing market," Stephen Stanley, chief economist at Pierpont Securities, said in a note to clients.

But some of the figures suggest more than just the weather may be at work.

The pace of housing starts last month actually rose in the chilly Northeast by 61.9%. But it fell 67.7% in the Midwest to the lowest pace on record. Starts also fell 12.5% in the South and 17.4% in the West, which has experienced relatively warm weather.

"While we believe the weather did impact construction activity, we think this is just part of the story, as the new construction slowdown was more broad-based," BNP Paribas economist Yelena Shulyatyeva wrote in a note to clients.

Building permits were still up 2.4% in January from a year earlier, though housing starts last month were down 2% from a year ago.

The report prompted several economists to lower their predictions for first-quarter economic growth. Macroeconomic Advisers cut its forecast by a tenth of a percentage point to an annualized 1.7%. Barclays Capital lowered its forecast by 0.3 percentage point to an annual rate of 1.9%.

As winter weather abates, "we expect that some of the softness in private residential construction will be reversed in the coming months, most likely in the second quarter," Barclays senior U.S. economist Michael Gapen wrote in a note to clients.

The U.S. housing market's recovery showed signs of cooling in the second half of 2013 as prices rose and the Federal Reserve began pulling back its bond-buying program, which is aimed at lowering borrowing costs. A 30-year, fixed-rate mortgage had an average interest rate of 4.43% in January, up from last May's average of 3.54%, according to Freddie Mac.

PulteGroup Inc., one of the largest U.S. home builders, budgeted $2 billion for spending on land this year, up from $1.3 billion in 2013. "Our decision to increase investment into the business is consistent with our view that U.S. housing continues to advance to a sustained multiyear recovery," Chairman and Chief Executive Richard Dugas said on a Jan. 30 earnings call.

But home-builder confidence took a hit in February, falling to its lowest level since May, according to a National Association of Home Builders survey released Tuesday.

"Significant weather conditions across most of the country led to a decline in buyer traffic last month," Kevin Kelly, the trade group's chairman, said in a statement.

"Builders also have additional concerns about meeting ongoing and future demand due to a shortage of lots and labor."

Lowe's Cos . said Wednesday it plans to hire about 25,000 seasonal employees at its U.S. stores during its spring busy season, down from 45,000 workers last year.

Lowe's and peer Home Depot have been rare bright spots in the retail sector as they continue to benefit from the U.S. housing recovery. Rising home prices and improvements in home construction and new home sales have led homeowners to open their wallets for remodeling projects or big-ticket items.

Lowe's said it plans to hire and train new seasonal employees first in areas where the climate has begun to warm, and continue on a market-by-market basis by climate and geography. Hiring has already begun in Florida, south Texas, Arizona and Southern California, it added.

Lowe's operates more than 1,825 home improvement and hardware stores in the U.S., Canada and Mexico.

Lowe's stock fell 0.9% Wednesday to trade at $46.54. The stock is down about 7.7% in the last three months.

Markets
Cold Weather Presents Quandary for Investors; The Big Questions: How Much Is Winter Chilling the Economy, and How to Shelter Portfolios
Alexandra Scaggs
899 words
19 February 2014
13:51
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

"Can we stop talking about the weather?"

That plaintive cry from Federated Investors analysts in their weekly email to clients Tuesday echoed the feeling of many weary traders and investors over the past few weeks.

But the brutal freeze affecting much of the U.S. presents more than just fodder for idle small talk in financial markets these days. It also presents an unexpected conundrum for many investors: Will the subzero wind chills and snowstorms have a lasting effect on the economy? And if so, which stocks should investors buy and sell?

"The amount of conversations with clients about this weather theme has reached a fever pitch," said Joseph Spinelli, who heads cash equity trading for the Americas at Deutsche Bank's New York office.

For Mr. Spinelli, that has meant being in regular contact with the bank's own energy analysts—who are plugged into the weather because of its impact on commodity prices—for insights into historical weather patterns and their impact on certain sectors. "It's the topic du jour," he said.

The weather has been blamed for two weak jobs reports in a row as well as poor retail sales, construction and manufacturing data. With plenty of winter left, the weather could be a factor to contend with for weeks, or even months, before any distortions are reversed.

For now, many investors take the view that those weak economic trends will reverse when the thaw comes. But for others, the cold snap has been a reason to change gears in portfolios that had been betting on stronger economic growth.

Huntington Asset Advisors Inc. has been cutting back exposure to subsectors like auto makers, glass manufacturers and home builders because of the weather, said Randy Bateman, chief investment officer. Huntington has been selling shares of those stocks or hedging against declines with options strategies, he said.

"The weather is going to affect auto sales, retail sales, employment…it's certainly going to affect the housing area," said Mr. Bateman, whose firm manages $14.5 billion in Columbus, Ohio.

One result of the bad weather has been the kind of blizzard seen only on Wall Street: research reports. Deutsche Bank this week published a report titled, "Winter's Worst Puts Pessimists in Full Bloom." BNP Paribas currency strategists joined in on Tuesday, saying the U.S. dollar outlook "remains clouded by weather."

Morgan Stanley's economic group, meanwhile, last week attempted to estimate what economic growth would look like were it not for the bad weather.

"The geographic and intramonth granularity in our primary datasets suggest a meaningful drag from weather in many key economic series," they wrote. The firm's "counterfactual" estimates included pending home sales rising 7.8% in January instead of 0.2% and retail sales gaining 0.5% excluding gasoline and auto sales, instead of declining 0.2%.

The weather has loomed large for investors because many started 2014 with high expectations for the economy. Many tilted their portfolios toward stocks that stood to benefit from a continued upswing in growth. Fed officials, too, expressed optimism about the economy as they have started to pare back their stimulus efforts.

That means a lot rides on whether the weather really is behind the U.S. economy's chill.

Eric Stein, co-director of the $13 billion global income group at Eaton Vance Corp., said for now, he and his team in Boston are treating the weather as "noise." He said some of the obsession with the weather stems simply from the financial-services sector's being concentrated in hard-hit areas like New York and other parts of the Northeast.

"To some extent, the people who are in the financial business kind of overextrapolate what's happening to them to everyone else," he said.

Eric Marshall, portfolio manager with the $1.8 billion Hodges Funds, decided to try and take advantage of that noise. He bought shares of cement and concrete maker Eagle Materials Inc. as the shares slid after the company reported that fourth-quarter earnings and sales fell short of analysts' forecasts.

Mr. Marshall said the cold weather likely weighed on demand for building materials such as cement and he expects sales to rebound in the spring. "Weather…is temporary in nature [and] usually creates a good buying opportunity," he said.

Even for some traders in the energy markets, where prices routinely rise and fall based on expectations for winter heating demand, the weather-driven swings have been wearing.

Natural-gas futures have not only surged this winter, they have also been highly volatile. So far this year, they have moved at least 5% during 13 trading sessions. They shot to a more than five-year high of $6.149 per million British thermal units Wednesday.

Jaya Bajpai, executive director of Applied Energy Analytics LLC, a Boston money-management and energy consulting firm, often places bets on the short-term direction of natural-gas prices. But the huge and erratic price swings have led him to back off.

"There are many ways for me to give myself heartburn, and trading a commodity that is going up and down 10% every day based solely on the latest weather model is not my preferred way of generating heartburn," he said.

Real Estate
Canada Seeks Soft Landing for Highflying Housing; Observers Say Market is Vulnerable to a Sharp Correction
Don Curren
1214 words
18 February 2014
13:11
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

TORONTO—Nearly a year after Canadian Finance Minister Jim Flaherty scolded the country's biggest banks for fueling record-high housing prices by cutting mortgage rates, his campaign to prevent a bubble is gaining traction.

Canada's fragile economy means the Bank of Canada can't safely raise interest rates, depriving policy makers of a traditional tool to keep the market from overheating. Instead, they have used a combination of tighter lending rules and verbal warnings, including the unusual rebuke of the banks in March 2013, to try to keep prices in check.

While it isn't clear whether those moves have been enough, many credit Mr. Flaherty for taking some of the froth out of the market.

"The reality on the ground is that the government has definitely achieved a soft landing in housing," said David Rosenberg, chief economist at Toronto money manager Gluskin Sheff + Associates Inc.

But many observers, including prominent economists such as Nouriel Roubini, think the market is still drastically overvalued and vulnerable to a sharp correction. The ultimate outcome of Mr. Flaherty's bid to rein in prices without raising interest rates could be an important test of the ability of policy makers in Canada and elsewhere to avoid a repeat of the housing bust that contributed to the global financial crisis.

The Canadian housing market's expansion over the past dozen years has led to bloated personal debt and a construction industry that accounts for almost twice as big a percentage of Canada's gross domestic product as the sector does in the U.S.

Canadian home prices have doubled since 2002 to an average of 388,553 Canadian dollars (US$353,873) in January, rising steadily except for a brief pause during the recession. Current prices are roughly 50% above those in the U.S.

Citing the risks of Canada's housing market, some global investors have trimmed their exposure to Canada's banking industry or taken short positions in the sector to protect themselves against any correction.

The next few months may bring more clarity about the market's condition as data become available on the sales and prices in the spring, the crucial season in Canada's market.

Meanwhile, house prices have risen strongly in several other developed countries—including Sweden, Norway, Australia and New Zealand—in part because easy money makes mortgages cheap.

In Australia, home prices in major cities have soared as the central bank has sought to keep rates low to cushion the economy from the end of a long mining boom. New Zealand's central bank tightened lending rules last fall after it said a hot housing market was the biggest threat to the country's financial stability.

Canada's central bank has kept rates low to help nurture an economy struggling with sluggish exports and sputtering business investment.

Instead, the Canadian government tightened mortgage rules four times in four years, the last time in July 2012. That last round had an initial impact of slowing the pace of sales and price increases in late 2012 and early 2013.

From 2008 to 2012, Mr. Flaherty implemented several changes, including reducing the maximum amortization period for a government-insured mortgage to 25 from 40 years, and lowering the upper limit that Canadians can borrow against their home equity to 80%. The changes helped generate a respite in housing activity as some buyers were forced to wait on the sidelines.

Then Mr. Flaherty took the extraordinary step last year of publicly rebuking Canada's major banks for cutting fixed mortgage rates to near-record lows, a move that drew the ire of at least one bank chief.

But since last summer, prices have roared back and Mr. Flaherty hasn't ruled out further action. Average home prices in Canada rose 9.5% in January from the same month in 2013, according to the country's real-estate trade group.

Deutsche Bank said in December that Canada's housing market was 60% overvalued after ranking several countries' markets according to an average of two ratios: house prices to rent and house prices to income. Belgium and New Zealand weren't far behind.

Still, not everyone believes the housing market's climb means it is at a dangerous point. Canadian banks tend to see a modest domestic overvaluation, with Toronto-Dominion Bank putting it at about 7%.

Bank of Canada Governor Stephen Poloz recently told lawmakers he didn't see a bubble. But he also has echoed his predecessor, the current Bank of England Governor Mark Carney, stating there was a risk that housing prices could continue to climb sharply and "set the stage for a sharp correction down the road."

The central banker is in a difficult position as he confronts household debt and the housing market. With Canada's economy growing at a lackluster pace, the central bank recently dropped a long-standing bias toward higher rates and shifted to a neutral position on monetary policy. The Bank of Canada's benchmark rate has stood at 1% for more than three years.

Mr. Rosenberg—the former Merrill Lynch chief economist who became known for forecasting the U.S. housing collapse—said that while housing prices remain high, Canada's six-month supply of housing based on current sales suggests that prices will remain stable. Canadian builders have curbed production more quickly than U.S. builders did before the bust there; some markets, such as luxury condos in Toronto and Vancouver, are more vulnerable to steeper corrections, he said.

Property developers, particularly those in robust markets such as resource-rich Alberta, remain positive about the market, but say they are aware of the possible effects of high prices.

Jim Bryce, vice president of sales at the Carlisle Group, a Calgary condo developer, said demand for new housing projects remains strong in Alberta, a direct result of the province's booming oil and gas industry. But he notes that some buyers are being priced out of the market, and as a result Carlisle has focused on small, more-affordable projects. "From a construction standpoint, we're looking good," Mr. Bryce said. "We think a minimum price increase between 6% to 8% is very realistic this year."

While the tighter mortgage rules may have discouraged some buyers, many continue to be enticed by low rates.

Lauren Posen, a Toronto-based recruiter at a software company, is looking for her first home, eyeing a condominium in the downtown core of Canada's largest city. Ms. Posen says she isn't that worried about a crash, nor does she feel much pressure to get into the market before mortgage rates climb higher. Instead, she is taking her time, looking to find a condo that suits her needs—a one-bedroom unit within walking distance of her downtown office and close to a subway line.

"You don't get much for C$500,000 in downtown Toronto anymore," she said. The average cost of a condo in the downtown Toronto core in the fourth quarter last year was C$440,966, up 7.5% from the same period in 2012, according to the city's real-estate board.

Markets
Central Banks Walk Policy Tightrope; Efforts to Safeguard Economies Risk Causing New Distortions
Richard Barley
512 words
17 February 2014
02:16
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

It's a fine line to walk. Central banks in many developed economies are holding rates near zero to protect fragile economies. But they are also rightly wary about the potential for those low rates to stoke new distortions, such as housing bubbles.

The apparent answer to this challenge is so-called macroprudential policy, seen as a targeted way to intervene to rein in imbalances such as excessive lending via regulatory measures. The Bank of England has made clear that this, rather than the blunt tool of a rate increase, is the preferred option in dealing with excesses in a revitalized housing market; the Swiss National Bank is already trying out tweaking capital requirements to manage the risks in its real-estate market.

But will it work? The BOE points to examples such as changes to risk weightings for commercial real estate in India in 2005-2006, or to car loans in Brazil in 2010, where there was a large reduction in lending. That suggests that very targeted measures can work.

But while macroprudential policy might slow lending growth, it may not have the same clout as an interest-rate hike in changing borrowers' behavior.

In Sweden, the Financial Supervisory Authority noted in December that despite higher capital requirements for banks, lending has continued to grow, while the impact on lending rates had been small; Swedish household debt stands at above 170% of disposable income. The Riksbank says knowledge about the effectiveness of macroprudential policy is still limited.

In Switzerland, the SNB has had to launch a second round of increases to the capital required to be held against mortgages. So far, the effect of macroprudential policy there appears limited. UBS's Swiss Real Estate Bubble Index continued to climb into the "risk" category in the fourth quarter; house prices are growing faster than income and mortgage debt is now around 110% of gross domestic product. Swiss fixed-interest mortgage rates have risen only 0.08-0.09 percentage point, notes Nomura. Talks are now under way on further measures that may include tightening lending criteria.

The risk is that consumers may only respond to something that puts clear limits on what they can borrow, such as caps on loan-to-value ratios, or debt-service ratios. These would undoubtedly be powerful, but could also be controversial. The BOE has said it thinks there needs to be a broad public debate about LTV restrictions for them to be acceptable.

The key issue here may well be one of signaling. An announcement that a central bank is requiring banks to raise their countercyclical capital buffers, or that it is changing sectoral capital requirements, is clearly going to be more difficult to explain than a rise in a country's interest rate. And it will take a lot to drown out the signal that rates at historical lows send to housing markets and home buyers. The tightrope central banks are trying to walk may be a perilous one.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

U.K. House Prices Rise Again in February; February's Monthly Increase was Strongest Since October 2012, Says Rightmove
Ilona Billington
699 words
16 February 2014
22:24
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LONDON—U.K. house prices accelerated further in February, a survey showed Monday, as demand continues to outpace supply, a trend that looks set to continue for some time.

Since the government's 'Help to Buy' program opened for business in April 2013, the U.K.'s housing market has surged. Not only have prices gained upward momentum, but house sales and mortgage approvals have also increased.

That run of price gains extended into February, according to online estate agency Rightmove, which said the average asking price of £251,964 ($420,629) in February was 3.3% or £8,103 higher than in January. The monthly increase was the strongest since October 2012, while on the year prices increased 6.9% from February 2013, the sharpest gain since November 2007.

In January average asking prices rose 1.0% on the month and by 6.3% year-over-year.

"The market rebound continues," said Miles Shipside, Rightmove's director and housing market analyst. "New sellers are now asking over £16,000 more than those who came to market a year ago, a rate of increase not seen since before the credit-crunch took hold in 2008."

The further increase in asking prices comes despite an 18% increase in the number of homes advertised for sale on the Rightmove website. That highlights the strength of demand for homes, something that has been fueled by accelerating demand from the 'Help to Buy' program which now provides mortgage guarantees to first-time buyers of newly built homes, all-buyers of existing stock up to the price of £600,000 and is now available to Islamic buyers.

Indeed, data shows that average house prices rose more than £10,000 between April 2013 and January this year, according to lender Nationwide's figures. And, mortgage approvals as measured by the Bank of England were almost 20,000 higher over a similar period.

That pace of increase is notable, and being fed by more first-time buyers with small deposits, suggests that some mortgage approvals could be riskier than the new rules suggest.

"People, particularly young people are starting to feel as though now is the right time to buy and that makes me a little uncomfortable," Kate Barker, a former member of the Bank of England's Monetary Policy Committee and author of the government's 2006 'Review on Housing', told The Wall Street Journal.

"The government's schemes make it seem that now is the time to get into the market, so some people are stretching themselves, not only to become homeowners, but also to make money as the expectation is that house prices will continue to rise," Ms. Barker, also currently a senior policy adviser for Credit Suisse, said.

Ms. Barker added that while she has some concerns overall she is in agreement with the Bank of England's view that we're not in a bubble at the moment.

Rightmove's Mr. Shipside, meanwhile, told The Wall Street Journal that the current fast pace of house price inflation is largely down to government schemes enabling a release in pent up demand.

"'Help to Buy' has given lenders confidence, and overall its good for the economy to have a functioning housing market," Mr. Shipside said.

A healthy housing market is indeed key to a growing economy, as consumers move home they spend money which helps to power economic growth and boost business confidence and employment.

But, with supply still below demand further upward momentum in house price growth is pretty certain, and that should set alarm bells ringing, despite the new era of sensible and stricter mortgage lending.

"The shortage of property in the U.K. has been going on for some 20 years and is supporting demand," Mr. Shipside said.

Meanwhile, BOE Governor Mark Carney and other BOE members have stated on numerous occasions that while the broad picture of the housing market isn't consistent with a bubble forming, the bank remains vigilant over house prices and ready to intervene if and when it becomes necessary to do so.

Ports, Shipping Companies Retool for Bigger Panama Canal; Global Investments Hang in the Balance as Contract Dispute Stalls Project
Dan Molinski
1233 words
17 February 2014
11:14
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

PANAMA CITY—Shipbuilders are cranking out ever-bigger container vessels and the world's major ports are dredging deeper, all on account of an ambitious multibillion-dollar project to widen the Panama Canal—an endeavor currently stalled by a contract dispute.

More than just the canal is tangled up in the acrimonious battle between Panama and the builders over $1.6 billion in cost overruns.

"There are many cities, countries and port authorities who are spending billions of dollars in anticipation of the traffic that will come from that newly expanded canal," said Adam Putnam, Florida's commissioner of agriculture. "So, there will be an impact if there is an extended delay."

The dispute so far has halted construction for two weeks and now threatens to delay the project by at least three years beyond the proposed completion date of December 2015. Negotiators say they are hopeful of a settlement as soon as Tuesday.

Still, even if resolved, the dispute underscores the difficulties and costs of altering long-range plans, and leaves players throughout the world wondering if further problems will arise in a complex project that already has been delayed by more than a year.

The booming U.S. liquefied natural gas industry has been eagerly awaiting the chance to ship exports to Asia on its huge ships via a bigger canal.

Miami, meanwhile is spending hundreds of millions of dollars to deepen its harbor and build an underwater tunnel for trucks carrying goods for bigger ships that would use the new canal. Among other industries, citrus growers in Florida are seeking to get their products to Asia and the Pacific coast of Latin America on bigger ships at lower cost, by using a refurbished canal.

Plans for the canal expansion prompted the shipping world to accelerate the building of larger container vessels, said Jonathan Roach, a senior container analyst at Braemar Seascope, a London chartering and ship-broking company.

Orders have been placed for 214 of the so-called neo-Panamax ships—which are as long as four football fields and more than 160 feet high— with sights set on a wider, deeper canal, according to Alphaliner, a shipping-industry trade publication.

Smaller ships are being taken out of service. So far this year, 14 of the older model Panamax vessels—the biggest shipping vessels now using the canal, holding 4,500 containers—have been scrapped and 40 will be turned into scrap metal by year-end, Mr. Roach said. Last year, only 19 were scrapped. Replacing them are the neo-Panamax vessels, capable of carrying as many as 13,200 containers, each 20 feet in length.

"Plans to replace the Panamaxes are well on the way despite the expected delays," Mr. Roach said.

With those bigger ships in mind, 10 of the largest East Coast and Gulf Coast ports in the U.S. are planning projects costing more than $11 billion to dredge deeper, upgrade terminals and add supersize cranes over the next several years.

The LNG industry in the U.S. is counting on a larger canal to speed up delivery to energy-hungry Asia, because most LNG ships today are too big for the current Panama canal. Once the canal is enlarged, though, 90% of the tankers carrying the liquefied gas would be able to use the waterway, according to a November study by the U.S. Department of Transportation. The canal connects the Caribbean with the Pacific and saves ships an 5,000-mile voyage.

"It's very important to us to see the canal completed," said Bill Cooper, president of the Center for Liquefied Natural Gas, a Washington-based trade association. "Otherwise you're adding a significant amount of time to go all the way around South America, and a significant cost."

In 2007, Panamanian voters approved the expansion under the premise that revenue from tolls, now more than $1 billion yearly, would double or triple with the arrival of larger ships crossing the 50-mile waterway.

Channel dredging, carried out by U.S. and other foreign contractors at a cost of about $2.1 billion, went smoothly. But then on New Year's Day, the European consortium widening the canal said it needed $1.6 billion more on top of the $3.1 billion that had been budgeted for the most expensive part of the project, the construction of locks on either end of the canal.

After weeks of haggling, the consortium led by Spain's Sacyr SA and Italy's Impregilo SpA is closer to reaching a deal that hinges on Spain's government supporting Sacyr with a hefty loan, said a person close to the talks.

In one sign of the delay's impact, unknown future costs for transporting LNG to Asia are prompting potential buyers in Asian markets to put off signing long-term contracts with LNG exporters, said Jamie Welch, the chief financial officer for Energy Transfer Partners LP, which plans to convert its Lake Charles, La., facility to export LNG.

"I think what hurts the most is the longer it becomes protracted, the worse it will be," said Mr. Welch, referring to the talks between the builders and Panama's government.

Some ports, such as those in Baltimore and Norfolk., Va., were already deep enough for the neo-Panamax vessels. Others, including ports in New York and Miami, were racing to be ready by late 2015, jockeying to win more traffic in the expected shuffling of trade routes that the larger ships are expected to trigger.

The idea was to cement business deals before other ports caught up.

"We thought we had a window of seven to 10 years to just make hay of our natural assets," said Joe Harris, spokesman for the Port of Virginia, which can already take neo-Panamax vessels. "Maybe that window is shortened a little bit."

Jacksonville, Fla., Savannah, Ga., and Charleston, S.C., could benefit, analysts said, because they will now have more time to catch up to ports that began to expand earlier. Savannah is hoping to dredge to 47 feet by late 2017, two years beyond the date canal officials say they hope to finish. Charleston is aiming to reach 50 feet by late 2018.

In Miami, multiple interrelated projects—the underground highway to the port, dredging from 42 feet to 50 feet, and the installation of new cranes—will cost $2 billion. All of it is carefully timed to the completion of the canal's expansion.

Just days before the delay in Panama began, Bill Johnson, PortMiami's director, touted the foresight of preparing for bigger ships "as the model success story in the nation." In the midst of the delays, he said that "if it drags on for years and years, that's not good."

There is a sense among those closely following the delays that no matter what happens, ever-larger ships will dominate and a deeper, widened canal will be part of the equation.

"It's too important to the world," said Curtis Foltz, executive director of the Georgia Ports Authority. "It is going to happen."

Corrections & Amplifications The first name of Jamie Welch, chief financial officer for Energy Transfer Partners LP, was given incorrectly given as Jaime in a previous version of this article.

VOLKSWAGEN

Union Vote Begins

At Plant in Tennessee

Voting in a key labor election got under way on Wednesday at Volkswagen AG's plant in Chattanooga, Tenn., as the president of the United Auto Workers union said the union would be "deeply involved" in the plant if workers accept its representation.

Volkswagen is interested in having the UAW represent its workers to enable the plant to establish a works council, a committee of blue and white collar employees who negotiate work conditions with management. It appears that a works council is possible under U.S. law only if workers are represented by an outside union.

If the UAW prevails in the vote, it would cede some of the negotiating responsibilities it normally has to the plant's works council.

Nevertheless, "we're not really giving up control," UAW President Bob King said in an interview with CNBC.

A spokesman for the National Labor Relations Board, which is running the election, said the change in shift times should not affect voting. Voting stations are scheduled to be open this afternoon from 3 p.m. until 8:30 p.m. and again from 11 p.m. until 11:45 p.m.

-- Neal E. Boudette

ROLLS-ROYCE

Probe at Engine Maker

Leads to Two Arrests

The U.K.'s Serious Fraud Office on Wednesday said it had searched a number of premises and made two arrests in connection with its investigation into allegations of bribery and corruption at engine maker Rolls-Royce Holdings PLC.

The arrests and searches are the latest development in a year-long probe that kicked off when Rolls-Royce said it had passed information to the SFO relating to concerns about bribery and corruption in Indonesia and China.

The maker of engines for military and commercial jets and ships said in December 2012 that internal "investigations had identified matters of concern in these, and in other overseas markets," and warned of possible consequences including the prosecution of individuals and the company.

The company declined to comment Wednesday.

Separately Wednesday, Rolls-Royce said it reached an agreement with Lockheed Martin Corp. valued at up to $1 billion to deliver around 600 engines for Lockheed's C-130J military transport planes. The agreement will service U.S. government and international contract requirements between 2014 and 2018.

-- Marietta Cauchi

HOME DEPOT

An Expected Busy Spring

Increases Seasonal Hiring

Home Depot Inc. said Wednesday it would hire 80,000 workers in the U.S. as it prepares for spring, its busiest sales period, matching last year's hiring level.

Home Depot and peer Lowe's Cos. have been a rare bright spot in the retail sector as they continue to benefit from the U.S. housing recovery. Rising home prices and improvements in home construction and new home sales have led homeowners to open their wallets for remodeling projects or big-ticket items.

The home-improvement retailer said jobs are available on a market-by-market basis, based on individual store needs and geographical variance in climate.

Last week the company said it would hire 6,300 associates in Canada for the spring, down from last year's plan to hire 6,700 employees.

Home Depot currently employs more than 300,000 associates.

WHOLE FOODS MARKET

Grocer Trims Forecasts

For a Second Time

Whole Foods Market Inc. trimmed its profit and sales forecasts for the second time in four months, as the high-end grocer struggles to compete in the wobbly economy.

The Austin, Texas, company said on Wednesday it now expects sales growth of 11% to 12% for fiscal year ending in September, from its previous forecast of 11% to 13% growth. Its closely watched same-store sales growth is expected to be 5.5% to 6.2% this year, compared with its historical average of 7% to 8%.

For its fiscal first quarter ended Jan. 19, Whole Foods' earnings rose 8.2% and comparable-store sales were up 5.4%.

For the year, Whole Foods expects per-share earnings of $1.58 to $1.65, compared with its prior forecast of $1.65 to $1.69.

For the quarter ended Jan. 19, Whole Foods reported a profit of $158 million, or 42 cents a share, compared with $146 million, or 39 cents a share, a year earlier.

Revenue increased nearly 10% to $4.24 billion.

Buying a Vacation Home in Fiji; The island nation has hundreds of islands, empty beaches, friendly locals and gentle prices, but buying in this corner of Polynesia has its challenges
Tamara Audi
1438 words
13 February 2014
14:32
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Two years ago, not long after Paul and Kate Anderson and their family arrived at their open-air, glossy-wood beachfront home in Fiji, their daughter and her boyfriend went down the back steps to the sand. The pair returned to announce their engagement; within the hour, the couple's son and his girlfriend were engaged too.

"This place is magical," said Kate Anderson, 59 years old, gazing at her perfectly framed view of palm fronds and sapphire-blue sea. The Andersons, who get to Fiji via a four-hour flight from Australia, paid $1 million in 2009 to buy the land and build their 6,400-square-foot, five-bedroom home in the Maui Bay Estates development on Viti Levu, Fiji's largest and most populated island.

For centuries, this collection of 333 South Pacific islands with a population just over 896,000 has lured adventurers, mercenaries and evangelists (some of whom ended their travels as meals for local cannibals). Now, Fiji attracts an eclectic mix of Australians, New Zealanders, diving enthusiasts and beach-loving romantics who are drawn to its steamy jungles, colorful underwater vistas and endless supply of white-sand seclusion.

After a few difficult years following the country's 2006 military coup and the global economic slowdown, Fiji is back on the upswing. In 2012, more than 660,000 people visited Fiji—a 20% increase over 2006, according to Fiji's Bureau of Statistics.

And while there are real challenges to buying or building a second home here—including legal issues, corruption and bumpy logistics—many people are jumping in. Along with the return of Australians, New Zealanders and Americans, cash buyers are coming from new places such as China and Papua New Guinea.

"We're seeing a real upswing," said David Miller, director of Taveuni Development, which has developments on three islands, including Maui Bay Estates. The company, which usually sells about 50 lots a year at prices ranging from $35,000 to $475,000, sold 12 lots in January alone—"a record for us."

Fiji fans praise the nation's particular brand of friendliness—Fijians are known for inviting visitors to stay at their homes—and its jungles that creep right to the water's edge. Fiji is "like what the Bahamas was maybe 20 years ago," said Traviss Orr, head of sales for Naisoso Island, a new gated residential and resort development taking shape on a narrow island connected to Viti Levu by a bridge. Prices range from $270,000 for a quarter-acre lot to $700,000 for a half-acre lot.

Then there is the cost, which is substantially lower than that for many other tropical destinations. One-acre beachfront lots at Nanuku Resort and Spa, for example, will sell for approximately $1 million, a fraction of the prices in Hawaii.

Chris and Nicola Alpe, 34, who live in Australia and Los Angeles, are part-owners of Vomo Island Resort, a tiny island with 28 luxury villas that cost about $1,000 to $1,300 a night. Now, they're spending $1 million to build a three-bedroom, 5,000-square-foot vacation home on an acre of beachfront that they hope to rent when they aren't using it, for $3,000 to $5,000 a night.

"You'll have a picnic, and you can strip off and go snorkeling and never see another person all day," said Mr. Alpe, 63, who proposed to his wife on a small deserted island nearby.

The couple's investment illustrates an important feature of Fijian real estate: Foreigners can't purchase most of its land outright. The former British colony set aside 90% of its land for native ownership—land controlled by Fijian clans who can lease the land to foreigners. So the Alpes paid $600,000 for an acre of leased land; the lease gives them possession of their acre for 99 years—the typical time frame.

Once the contract is up, the terms can be renegotiated. About 6% of Fiji's land is freehold—it can be purchased outright by foreigners.

Fijian developers and land experts said leases are generally stable, but can become problematic if either side decides to renegotiate the terms or if a dispute develops. Also, while many foreigners build their own homes—the country's modern luxury stock is low and much of its prime beachfront is undeveloped—experts said it is unclear who ultimately owns the homes if a lease is broken or ends.

Fiji's political situation also is in flux. The country is run by the military commander who orchestrated a coup. Corruption is an issue. The government has improved the country's legal protections and recently established a real estate licensing board.

Still, "you can't fix it overnight," said Ken Chambers, a land-management expert in Fiji. The Fiji Independent Commission Against Corruption was set up in 2007 to investigate and prosecute government corruption, according to government officials.

Ruth and David Peterson, retirees from outside Seattle, Wash., spent $1.1 million to purchase freehold land and build their 5,500-square-foot home at Maui Bay in 2007. They said the owners of the neighboring property started using it as a guesthouse for tourists, and the Petersons have since been locked in a battle with the government and the neighbors. "It's been a nightmare," Ruth Peterson said, adding that she doesn't like the noise and disruption of tourists coming and going.

Annemarie DeVos, who owns the neighboring property, said she received permission from the government to open a small boutique hotel on the site before she purchased and built the property in 2005. An employee with the Department of Town and Country Planning, which handles zoning requests, declined to discuss specific applications but said individual property owners can apply to the government directly for special use zoning, including for tourism.

Mr. Chambers, the land-management expert, said potential buyers should hire a Fiji-based lawyer and check that the title includes easements and restrictive covenants about what can and can't be built around them. Finding a reputable lawyer can be difficult; buyers should research the lawyer's reputation and track record.

Russell Pflueger, a 50-year-old developer of medical devices, and David Kohler, a 53-year-old owner of a sports memorabilia company, and their wives bought a 4,500-square-foot home on freehold land for $2.5 million in 2006, as part of a development that went bankrupt. The lenders, Chicago's Bixby Bridge Capital, took over and invested another $15 million to finish and improve the resort and residential development, rebranding it Nanuku Resort and Spa.

"It was unsettling," said Orange County, Calif.-based Mr. Pflueger, who with the others gets to Fiji via an 11-hour direct flight from Los Angeles. But the new owners "have done an amazing job."

Westerners also caution newcomers about Fiji's island culture, which doesn't always meet Western expectations of planning and punctuality.

Originally from northern California, Bob Facciola, a 77-year-old former owner of a wholesale food-distribution company, and Marie Peyman, a 65-year-old former professional horse trainer, spent $1 million on an acre of freehold land and are building a retreat with separate, free-standing bedroom suites.

The home, at the tip of the island between an ocean inlet and the Pacific, has soaring ceilings framed in timber lashed together with traditional Fijian materials.

The pair stressed the importance of finding a builder with experience in Fiji and who plans ahead—ordering materials and supplies months in advance to avoid cyclone season and shipping delays—and can manage complex sourcing. The couple hired a U.S.-trained Fijian architect and a New Zealand builder who relocated to Fiji. The total estimated cost of the project is about $2.5 million.

The Andersons, whose children got engaged at their house, said they had to give up a planned infinity pool when they couldn't find a builder with the expertise.

The Andersons said they love Fiji's relaxed pace and exotic charms. As they spoke, their housekeeper, a local woman, turned out platters of warm, sticky-sweet pastries topped with freshly grated coconut.

On a recent evening, the South Pacific glimmered in their doorway. Soon it would be time for the Andersons favorite ritual: sunset gin and tonics on the deck, just for the two of them.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Beverly Hills Mansion With Bowling Alley Asks $55 Million; A Bel Air estate lists for $43 million, plus news about mystery writer Sue Grafton and dermatologist Nicholas Perricone
