The owners of a 25,000-square-foot Beverly Hills mansion spared no expense in building their home.

The Mediterranean-style house, now on the market for $55 million, comes with a single-lane bowling alley, according to the listing agents Justin Mandile and Mauricio Umansky of the Agency.

Built in 2010, the house has eight bedrooms, eight full bathrooms and four half-baths, Mr. Mandile said. In addition to the bowling alley, the gated estate has indoor and outdoor pools, a gym, a billiards room and a wine cellar. There are two elevators, one of which goes to the master suite. A master bedroom closet also contains a concealed doorway that leads to the bedroom next door, currently configured as a nursery.

Off the main kitchen is a prep kitchen, bedroom and a full bathroom intended for use by a private chef. Overlooking the pool area, which is lined with greenery including conifer trees, there's a large circular fire pit.

The property is owned by Elias Shokrian, a commercial-real-estate developer and investor. Mr. Mandile, a family friend, said when Mr. Shokrian and his wife Shirley bought the property in 1989, there was an older house on the site where they lived for years and raised their children. Recently, they tore the house down and built the existing structure. They are selling because their children are now grown, and the parents "don't really need all that room," Mr. Mandile said.

Mystery writer Sue Grafton has reduced the asking price on her Montecito, Calif., home to $9.6 million. The 2-acre estate was initially listed in August 2013 for $10 million.

The roughly 7,300-square-foot main house has four bedrooms, each with an ocean-view terrace. There's a separate building which Ms. Grafton used as an office for writing, but could be used as a guesthouse. A pool house is currently configured as a gym. Also in the pool area, two pergolas provide a shaded area for sitting. "It's a wonderful house for entertaining," said Ms. Grafton, 73 years old, who hosted her daughter's wedding on the property in 1996.

Ms. Grafton, best known for her "Alphabet Series" of novels including "'A' Is for Alibi" and her latest, "'W' is for Wasted," said she and her husband bought the property in 1995. But they recently decided to build a new house on a lot they owned next door, where they moved in 2010. The purchaser of this property will be her neighbor, Ms. Grafton said, quipping: "whoever buys the house can borrow sugar any time they want!"

The listing agents are Rebecca Riskin, Sarah Kelly and Dina Landi of Riskin Associates at Village Properties Realtors.

A Vero Beach, Fla., beachfront house owned by Nicholas Perricone, a dermatologist and founder of the eponymous skin-care company, is on the market for $16.995 million.

Located in the private John's Island gated community, the house has more than 14,000 square feet and five bedrooms, six full and two half baths. The 2.3-acre property has about 93 feet of frontage on the Atlantic, with a private wooden walkway leading from the pool area to the beach.

While there, Dr. Perricone said he spends most of his time sitting by the pool or on a second-floor terrace overlooking the ocean, where he likes to drink tea and listen to the waves.

"Most of the time, I sit outside," he said, adding that the house is "a place to really recharge my batteries."

Dr. Perricone said that while he believes it's important to get some sun to build up vitamin D, he avoids too much exposure to the Florida rays, often choosing to sit on one of the home's covered loggias.

Dr. Perricone paid $10 million in 2009 for the home, which had been built on spec in 2007. Dr. Perricone bought the house fully furnished in a navy-blue and white palette by Palm Beach designer Jack Fhillips for "a polo look." (Mr. Fhillips said the home has "a bit of a nautical, sea captain's motif.") Those furnishings are now being offered for sale along with the house. "I don't want to be bothered with moving furniture," the dermatologist said.

Dr. Perricone said he is selling because his business has kept him busier than expected, leaving him little time to visit the house. "I just never get to use it," he said. "I really wanted to use it more."

The listing agents are Cindy O'Dare and Clark French of Premier Estate Properties.

Doheny Jr., who said his parents bought this property in part because it had previously been used as a botanical garden and was planted with a number of exotic specimen trees. She spent much of her time working in the extensive gardens on the secluded estate, which has views of the Pacific, he said. Mr. Doheny, Sr. died in 2003 and his wife last year, their son said.

This area, inside the East Gate on Bel Air Road, is "the bull's-eye of Bel Air—the finest part," said Mr. Hilton. Mr. Hilton, whose grandfather founded the Hilton Hotel empire, lives three doors down from the Doheny estate.

Mr. Hilton shares the listing with Judy Feder, Jeff Hyland, Mich Rogers and Tyrone McKillen, all of Hilton & Hyland.

Poland Sacks Roads Agency Head; Government Blames Roads Chief for Building Delays
Marcin Sobczyk
525 words
13 February 2014
07:31
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WARSAW—The Polish government fired its roads agency director Thursday, blaming him for massive construction delays and tensions within the sector.

Lech Witecki was dismissed after six years in the job as the agency he headed failed to complete a number of projects on schedule, according to the government.

"The gravest mistakes were many delays and bad atmosphere in the agency's cooperation with contractors and the entire construction sector," said Deputy Prime Minister Elzbieta Bienkowska, who is in charge of allocating European Union funding and the country's transport infrastructure.

Ahead of Poland's new highway construction push in 2014-2020, the agency needs "fresh stamina," she said.

Decades of central planning left Poland with decrepit roads and no highways when Communism collapsed in Europe after 1989. The country now has nearly 2,700 kilometers of highways, but major areas and key cities still lie outside the network.

The roads agency recently said it would still need many months and, in some cases, years to complete sections of the country's highways that were supposed to open ahead of the UEFA European soccer championship finals held in Poland and Ukraine in 2012.

Mr. Witecki wasn't available for comment on Thursday. However, he said in a recent interview with The Wall Street Journal that some builders arrived in Poland unprepared and that his agency wasn't to blame for the financial problems of builders from other EU countries.

"Those companies began bidding aggressively after projects stopped in Spain," Mr. Witecki said. "Especially when dealing with companies that were doing projects here for the first time, the fundamental issue was a lack of progress immediately after the start of the project."

"After signing, it took three or four months to start work instead of one month, some contractors would lose the winter season and this translated into penalties for missing milestones," he said.

The government had a budget of 74 billion zlotys ($24 billion) for roads in 2007-2013, of which about 60% was financed by the EU from funds allocated for poorer member states. It hasn't yet provided details of the cost of constructing some 1,800 kilometers of new roads planned for 2014-2020, when it will have a budget of around $37 billion for all infrastructure projects.

The abundance of projects and money for Poland's road building has attracted many companies, mostly from within Poland but also Spain, Germany and Austria. However, some firms have run into financial difficulties, blaming their woes on Poland's rigid public procurement laws, thin margins and bureaucracy.

Competition is fierce, with just under 300 companies filing bids in recent years for contracts tendered by the government. But Mr. Witecki argued that only a minority of them have run into financial difficulty, without naming any firms.

"If someone asks why it's so bad, then I say that out of more than 170 projects over the past five years we had problems in 10 or 11 cases, while we've built more than 2,500 kilometers of roads," he said.

Falling Property Values Hint at Trouble on the Farm; Plummeting Prices for Corn Are Threatening a Yearslong Boom for U.S. Growers
Jesse Newman
1303 words
12 February 2014
18:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Broker Pat Karst thought the farm being auctioned late last month would be scooped up. The 98-acre plot was of decent quality, and the volunteer fire station in Arlington, Ind., where his firm was holding the sale, was packed with farmers.

Instead, the evening ended with the latest in a spate of failed auctions, after the top bidder dropped out far below the asking price. "The moral of the story is: unrealistic expectations from sellers and more caution on the side of the buyer," said Mr. Karst, who acknowledged he, too, thought the property would fetch a higher price than offered.

The flop reflects a broader turning point in one of the U.S.'s biggest recent asset booms. From 2009 to mid-2013, average prices for agricultural land in the U.S. rose by half, while in Iowa, Nebraska and some other Midwest farm states, prices more than doubled, according to U.S. Department of Agriculture data from last August. That helped fuel economic prosperity across the Farm Belt while stoking fears about a possible bubble.

Now there is mounting evidence the boom is fizzling out. Farmland prices in Iowa fell 3% over the second half of last year, and those in Nebraska fell 1%, according to estimates from the Farm Credit Services of America, an Omaha, Neb., lender that calculates weighted averages based on land quality. Reports from U.S. Federal Reserve Banks across the Midwest late last year showed prices flattening or slipping from the previous quarter. A monthly survey of Midwestern lenders by Omaha-based Creighton University in January found the outlook for farmland and ranchland prices was the weakest in more than four years.

Despite the falling property values, agricultural analysts say a repeat of past farm-belt collapses is unlikely. Farmer income is expected to remain strong and debt levels are low, according to USDA figures.

But prices have plunged for corn, a key U.S. crop. After rising to all-time highs in 2012—driven by growing demand and tight supply because of a historic drought—prices for the biggest U.S. crop dropped 40% last year, thanks to a record harvest of 14 billion bushels. The Federal Reserve warned in January that corn prices, then around $4.28 a bushel, won't cover farmers' anticipated cost of raising the crop this year. Prices have since climbed to about $4.40 a bushel, compared with about $8.31 in August 2012.

Soybeans, the nation's No. 2 crop, have also lost value. Meanwhile, with the Fed scaling back its stimulus efforts, buyers of U.S. farmland face the prospect of higher interest rates after years of cheap borrowing.

The shifts have forced farmers to recalculate the value of productive land. Greg Plunk, a third-generation Illinois farmer who added 80 acres to his farm over the past two years, said he would be more careful with further purchases. "Profits will be tighter, there's not going to be near the returns, and guys will have to be careful how much expenses they've got into an acre," said Mr. Plunk, 53 years old.

Falling land prices could cause economic ripples, curbing farmers' ability to borrow money to buy new acreage, crop supplies or machinery. Land secures many of those loans. Mark Jensen, chief risk officer at Farm Credit Services of America, said half of its $20 billion portfolio consists of real-estate loans secured by farmland. As credit quality deteriorates, farmers will use more land as collateral, he said.

A pullback in farmers' spending could curtail construction of grain bins and livestock facilities as well as purchases of new machinery. Tractor company Deere & Co. predicted Wednesday that sales of farm equipment in the U.S. and Canada this year would decline 5% to 10% from 2013.

The farmland boom began roughly a decade ago. Prices slumped briefly in 2009, amid the recession, then rebounded, thanks in part to historically low interest rates. Overall, values of some fertile Midwestern land nearly tripled over the past decade.

The recent turn has been abrupt. "There was lots of land around here selling for close to $10,000 an acre, but it's tapered off quickly," said Kevin Kremer, 56, an Indiana farmer who recently won a tract of land for roughly $8,800 an acre at an auction in Burnettsville, Ind.

Winter is typically the busiest time in the county-fairground buildings and American Legion halls where fast-talking auctioneers deal swaths of land. But this season has seen a growing number of "no sale" auctions. After the unsuccessful 98-acre auction by Mr. Karst last month, the land sold a week later for $880,000, about 10% less than what the sellers wanted.

In Iowa, the biggest corn and soybean-producing state, 6.7% of farmland auctions failed in 2013, more than double the 2012 percentage, according to Farm Credit Services of America. The total number of auctions fell 30%.

As land prices were soaring, some economists and lenders raised alarms. The Federal Advisory Council, a group of 12 banking leaders that advises the Fed, wrote a year ago that farmland prices were "veering further from what makes sense from a production standpoint," amounting to "a bubble resulting from persistently low interest rates." The same group noted this past December that "prices are flat and may be receding."

So far, the sputtering growth is prompting more caution than panic. But economists are watching closely. "The question is, if there's a fall, how fast the fall happens," said Nathan Kauffman, Omaha branch executive of the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City, who tracks farmland prices.

The economic picture in the Farm Belt is expected to worsen. The USDA forecast Tuesday that U.S. farm incomes will dive 27% this year from 2013, to $95.8 billion, which would be the lowest level since 2010. Last year's total was the highest since 1973 on an inflation-adjusted basis, but the continued slump in grain prices is expected to this year outweigh the benefits of having more corn and soybeans to sell. Still, even with the expected decline, the USDA reckons incomes will remain $8 billion above the previous 10-year average.

Michael Duffy, professor of economics at Iowa State University in Ames, Iowa, projects lower income for farmers could drive the price of farmland down 20% to 25% over the next several years.

Other observers point to factors that could cushion or reverse the market decline, including an unexpected resurgence in the price of corn and soybeans. Some buyers say they are waiting to pounce if prices fall, which also could help keep any decline from turning into a rout.

"We think this next 12 months is going to be the best window we've had in the past five years" to invest in farmland, said Greyson Colvin, managing partner at investment manager Colvin & Co., which owns about 7,000 acres of farmland.

Adding comfort, today's agricultural sector looks markedly different than it did during the last farmland bust, in the early 1980s. Then, a roughly 50% drop in land values and rising interest rates led to high levels of debt when viewed as a percentage of farmers' assets. That forced many farmers out of business and spurred failures among dozens of agricultural lenders. Farmers' debt-to-asset ratio is currently estimated at 10.3%, less than half what it was in 1985, according to the USDA.

But a sharp fall in property prices could affect a farmer's balance sheet, said Mr. Kauffman, the economist. "If land values do drop, that could have some important implications for solvency."

U.K. House Prices Continue to Rise in January; Supply of Homes for Sale Falls to More Than Four-Year Low
Ilona Billington
457 words
12 February 2014
14:01
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LONDON—A more than four-year low in housing for sale helped U.K. home prices to continue rising in January, according to a monthly survey issued Thursday.

The combination of increased mortgage availability and a drop in the supply of housing for sale meant demand to purchase a property remained strong in the first month of 2014.

The January house-price balance slid to +53 from December's +56, according to the Royal Institution of Chartered Surveyors monthly housing market survey.

The house-price balance is calculated by subtracting the number of surveyors who reported a fall in prices from those who said house prices rose.

The survey also showed that the average number of homes for sale per estate agency fell to 59 in January, the lowest level since mid-2009 and down from 60.4 in December.

"It's no secret that we are seeing prices go up in many parts of the country, driven largely by the lack of properties coming onto the market," said Peter Bolton-King, RICS global residential director.

"With more people now in a position to buy a home than at any point over the past few years, there are simply not enough properties to satisfy demand. The upshot of this is increasing prices in many areas and this looks set to continue for the foreseeable future," Mr. Bolton-King said.

The survey—which also shows surveyors continue to expect prices to rise over the coming year—remains in line with other housing market indicators and shows that while average prices are below the 2007 precrisis peak, they are rapidly moving toward that level.

While remaining vigilant over house-price developments, economists and policy makers, however, maintain that a house-price bubble isn't a threat at present.

That doesn't mean it won't happen though, and with the average house price just £8,000 ($13,159) below the 2007 peak, according to lender Nationwide's figures, it could be closer than expected.

The number of surveyors expecting an increase in properties for sale fell to a balance of +32 in January from +55 in December and +75 in November.

Despite that decline, the traditional Spring boost to the housing market may confound those expectations.

"Some of our members have told us that, as the daffodils come out, more vendors could be looking to test the market as the traditional spring bounce begins to take effect. This can only be good news for a market that has seen supply struggle for a long, long time," Mr. Bolton-King said.

U.S. News: Some Hot Housing Markets Cooled in Fourth Quarter
By Conor Dougherty
630 words
12 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Many of the hottest U.S. real-estate markets started to cool in last year's fourth quarter as higher interest rates and sticker shock pushed some buyers to the sidelines.

Overall, the median price of an existing home rose 10.1% in the fourth quarter to $196,900 from a year earlier, according to a report released Tuesday by the National Association of Realtors, which tracked 164 markets. In the third quarter the median price rose 12.5%.

Slightly less than three out of four markets saw year-over-year price gains in the fourth quarter and about a quarter saw double-digit percentage increases. In the third quarter, about 88% of markets saw year-over-year price increases, a third with double digits.

The largest gains continue to be in markets that were hard hit by the real estate bust and have seen a frenzy of investor interest during the past year. Many of those markets, however, have cooled a bit, which real-estate agents contend is a good thing because the price run-up had started to scare off buyers.

In Atlanta -- where 33.3% price growth in the fourth quarter compared with a year ago was the fastest in the nation, pushing the median cost of a home to $142,400 -- growth slowed from 41.8% in the third quarter over a year ago.

Several other cities that saw 20% or better growth in the fourth quarter, such as Los Angeles, Phoenix, Jacksonville, Fla., and Sacramento, Calif., saw price growth slow from the prior quarter.

"There was a marked slowdown in buyers wanting to make a purchase. Why are they taking a second look? In my view, it is largely due to affordability coming down sharply, especially in areas where price increases have been 20% or 30%," said Lawrence Yun, the NAR's chief economist. "Some (buyers) may be pushed out of the market. But even people who qualify are asking the question: 'With prices increasing that fast, can it reverse?' A sudden price change of that magnitude gives a pause to buyers."

On the other end of the spectrum, the most depressed markets continue to be across upstate New York and the industrial Midwest. In cities such as Elmira, N.Y., Fort Wayne, Ind., and Champaign, Ill., prices fell 9.5% or more.

The U.S. recovery in home prices continues to be propelled by the twin forces of tight inventory and a falling share of distressed sales that drag down area prices. Distressed sales -- foreclosures and short sales that usually are snapped up at a discount by neighbors -- accounted for 14% of the fourth-quarter sales, down from 24% a year earlier.

Inventory eked up to a 4.9-month supply at the current sales pace, from 4.8 months a year ago. A "balanced" market has somewhere around a 6-month supply of homes, NAR says.

The report also showed that while most U.S. home markets are affordable to households earning the median income, a number of select markets clustered around the West Coast have seen prices steeply outpace income growth.

Four of the nation's five most expensive markets were in California. The biggest affordability problems have been around the San Francisco Bay Area, where big gains in employment -- much of it in the booming technology sector -- have exacerbated a long-run housing shortage and pushed prices to the highest in the nation.

The San Jose metro area was the nation's most expensive in the fourth quarter, with a median single family home price of $775,000. SanFrancisco was second with a median home price of $682,400.

Home prices aren't the only thing rebounding amid the housing market recovery. Hundreds more home products were on display this week at the annual International Builders' Show in Las Vegas as builders, designers and architects prepare for a year of increasing construction, remodeling and home sales.

Sprinkled throughout the sprawling exhibit floor at the Las Vegas Convention Center were displays of upscale and unusual items ranging from 12-foot-long gas fireplaces to kid-size toilets. Attendees perused luxe wine cabinets, fancy faucets that display water temperature and the latest in home-automation systems.

All told, the Builders' Show alone attracted 1,220 exhibitors this year, up from 980 last year. And the show's combination this year for the first time with the Kitchen and Bath Industry Show brought a few hundred more vendors to the lineup.

Here is a look at a few offerings that caught our eye on the exhibit floor.

A house doesn't need logs to be a log cabin. Modulog Industries , a Portland, Ore., company founded by former logger Ron Berge, produces siding made of Western red cedar for the outside of a home, with log ends and custom-made trim. The kiln-dried panels, measuring 7 inches wide, 1½ inches thick and up to 16 feet long, affix to the house with stainless-steel staples. "You can't tell the difference until you walk inside the home," Modulog President James Iafrati says.

Price: $9,000 to $14,000 (typical house)

Here is a hot trend in contemporary design: linear fireplaces. Travis Industries of Mukilteo, Wash., offers gas and electric fireplaces stretching from 5 to 20 feet across. The company's da Vinci custom-made fireplaces, sold to home builders and architects, feature a crushed-glass floor, from which the flames emerge, and adjustable colored lighting. Its more modest Xtreme by Fireplace Xtrordinair spans 5 feet wide and 20 inches tall, with steel trim around its glass face. Its logs are hand-painted ceramic that looks like driftwood.

Price: $2,200 to $35,000

His BottleLicious uses a proprietary technique in which glass bottles are melted flat for use in wall-mounted art or as functional features, such as an ornate kitchen backsplash. Mr. Salazar makes each piece to buyer's specifications, using various colors, frames and bottles, including keepsakes provided by the buyer.

Price: $750 to $7,000

The tubs, as large as 2.5 feet by 5 feet, stand on steel legs so owners don't have to bend over to wash their companion. Price: $2,000 to $6,000

The New Rules of the Hamptons; Here is the latest lowdown on the second-home spot
Candace Taylor
834 words
6 February 2014
14:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

A sales boom is shifting some of the longstanding laws that governed the Hamptons, the pricey resort communities on the eastern end of Long Island.

Sales activity in the Hamptons is now on par with, or even higher than, the mid-2000s peaks. While prices are still below boom-time levels, most of the inventory from the recession has now been absorbed. And in some areas, "there are more buyers than sellers," says real-estate broker Andrew Saunders of Saunders & Associates.

As a result, buyers and renters are widening their search to areas previously viewed as less desirable. Even the once-titanic divide between north and south of Montauk Highway is less pronounced. Because of their proximity to the ocean, homes south of the highway have long been the most expensive, but houses to the north are now seeing more demand than in the past.

Here is a guide to the personality of the housing market in eight Hamptons villages and hamlets—from genteel Southampton to the new money mansions of Sagaponack.

SOUTHAMPTON Southampton Village, with 7 miles of beachfront on the Atlantic, has long been a bastion of old money.

In the estate section, grand historic homes sit on grassy lawns concealed by hedge rows to protect owners' privacy. But the requirement for a blue-blooded pedigree is fading, agents say, as long-standing Southampton families sell compounds to newcomers.

Summer rentals are expensive: Harald Grant of Sotheby's International Realty is listing an 18,000-square-foot rental on 10 acres in the estate section. The price: $900,000, for Memorial Day to Labor Day.

EAST HAMPTON Smaller than Southampton, East Hampton Village also has grand estates dating to the Victorian era. But while Southampton is known for being understated and discreet, East Hampton has a more artistic, glamorous vibe.

This is where celebrities come to nest: Martha Stewart, Ron Perelman, Jerry Seinfeld and Sean "Diddy" Combs. The village's Main Street has its own Tiffany & Co. A house on Further Lane closed for $62.5 million in May.

WAINSCOTT This hamlet borders sought-after Georgica Pond, site of the exclusive Georgica Association. Despite its small size, the hamlet is home to "tremendous wealth," says Judi Desiderio of Town & Country real estate, due in part to its proximity to East Hampton Airport—helpful for commuting by private plane.

Wainscott has only a few oceanfront lots—one is on the market for $23.5 million.

SAGAPONACK In the past two decades, Wall Streeters have discovered once-rural Sagaponack, where they've built sprawling mansions on former potato fields.

Sagaponack buyers tend to be younger, and their money newer, than those in Southampton or East Hampton. The village has almost no retail and few historic homes. But that doesn't deter buyers lured by the wide-open ocean views, which contrast sharply to the hedge rows found in other areas.

BRIDGEHAMPTON Also rural for most of its history, Bridgehampton is big with the horse crowd. A popular event each year is the Hampton Classic Horse Show.

Bridgehampton also is popular with Wall Street types, many of whom choose the area because it has less restrictive zoning than Sagaponack. "In Bridgehampton, you can create a much larger home on a smaller lot," says Mr. Saunders. The hamlet does have a small commercial strip with restaurants such as Bobby Van's Steakhouse.

WATER MILL Large estates were built on Mecox Bay in the early 1900s, but much of the hamlet was undeveloped. Now, there are many lavish new homes.

Water Mill is popular with executives and celebrities; residents include Jennifer Lopez.

Rentals range from a 3,400-square-foot house on an acre asking $50,000 for the summer, according to the real estate listings website StreetEasy.com, to a 10,000-square-foot home on 2.7 acres that asks $475,000.

AMAGANSETTCorcoran Group broker Susan Breitenbach refers to Amagansett as "the TriBeCa of the Hamptons." The hamlet has several popular eateries and bars like the Stephen Talkhouse nightclub, where Jon Bon Jovi has performed unannounced. Actress Scarlett Johansson bought a $2.2 million house in the Amagansett Dunes about a year ago.

Prices are a bit lower. According to StreetEasy, summer rents range from $18,000 for a three-bedroom beach cottage to $525,000 for a seven-bedroom, 9½-bathroom estate.

SAG HARBOR A once-gritty former whaling village, Sag Harbor is now one of the most popular areas for dining and shopping.

"People are going to Sag Harbor who in the past would have ended up in East Hampton," said Noble Black, an agent at the Corcoran Group in Manhattan who paid $1.75 million in August for a home here.

With its narrow streets and boutiques, Sag Harbor is "a little bohemian, a little funky," says Ms. Breitenbach, the agent. "You're not going to see Tiffany's."

Square footage couldn't be determined but it is a full floor, the brokerage said.

Mr. Bronfman, chairman of the Seagram Co. and president of the World Jewish Congress, died in December at age 84. He lived in the apartment for about 40 years, said Brown Harris Stevens. The Bronfman family declined to comment. The listing agents are Mary Rutherfurd, Alina Pedroso and Leslie Coleman.

A 16,350-square-foot mansion in Barcelona, Spain, has recently listed for about $20 million.

Built on nearly 4 acres, the six-bedroom, eight-bathroom house has a billiards room, a swimming pool and sauna, a hot tub, a library and three gardens. Located in Sarrià, one of the most exclusive neighborhoods of the Spanish city, the 104-year old house has easy access to top schools and transport. It also enjoys some of Barcelona's best views of the sea and mountains.

Built between 1904 and 1910, the house is an example of Catalan modernism, an architectural style in vogue at the end of the 19th century that was characterized by an effort to emulate nature. The house was purchased in 1987 and restored by current owner Antoni Guinovart between 1989 and 1991. In 1993, he added parking and an elevator.

When restoring the house, Mr. Guinovart said he made sure he kept its modernist features. Mr. Guinovart said he followed the conventional wisdom that "less is more" when refurbishing the place because introducing other styles was "risky." However, the kitchen was modernized to make it "more practical, spacious and clean." A retired developer, Mr. Guinovart, 62, said he is selling because his kids have left home and he is looking to downsize.

Barcelona, along with the rest of the Spanish real-estate market, experienced a collapse of property prices on the back of the financial crisis. Even high-end products didn't completely escape, and some developments have seen price drops of nearly 40%. A recent forecast by PricewaterhouseCoopers and the Urban Land Institute has said the market is turning a corner and investor confidence has improved.

Harriet Beecher Stowe was the mother of six when she sat down to write the novel that would become "Uncle Tom's Cabin." According to Arline Pennell Lay, Ms. Stowe's home in Brunswick, Maine, was too noisy for her to get any work done. So the abolitionist rented a room in a nearby house. That is where, between 1850 and 1851, much of the novel was written in a room on the second floor, said Ms. Lay.

That nearby house, which is owned by Ms. Lay, is now on the market for $3 million with Teles Properties agents Ernie Carswell and Karen Nation. The family said receipts were found in the house showing rent that Ms. Stowe paid for her room.

The Lays said they have worked hard to preserve the historical character of their roughly 3,500-square-foot, six-bedroom, one-bath white clapboard house, built in the late 1700s. For that reason, there is only one bathroom, though a buyer could add a bathroom on the third floor.

The house has been in Ms. Lay's family since the early 1900s. Ms. Lay said she is selling because her husband died two years ago and the house is too big for one person. But the family wants to ensure that the buyer will keep the home intact.

One of the only townhouses on Manhattan's Central Park has been relisted for $29.95 million, or 19% less than its original asking price.

The home was listed this spring for $37 million by Sotheby's International Realty and was subsequently taken off the market after several price cuts. It is now co-listed by David Kornmeier of Brown Harris Stevens and Matthew Lesser of Leslie J. Garfield.

The landmark Queen Anne-style brick-and-limestone house is 22 feet wide with seven bedrooms, six full bathrooms and two half bathrooms, Mr. Kornmeier said. The home has about 10,745 square feet of living space. There is a finished basement with a gym and 60-foot-long lap pool. An elevator serves five levels. The home was one of nine constructed in 1888 by speculative builder William Noble, of which three remain.

The house is owned by Ukrainian businessman and philanthropist Igor Iankovsky, who paid $22.375 million in 2011 to purchase the property from former Coach President Keith D. Monda, according to public records. Mr. Monda, in turn, had purchased it from Abigail Disney, Walt Disney's grandniece, according to co-listing agent Mr. Lesser, who represented Mr. Iankovsky in his purchase.

Ms. Disney and Mr. Monda renovated and modernized the home, Mr. Lesser said, adding that the current price is "more in line" with the market than the original $37 million asking price. Mr. Iankovsky declined to comment.

Mr. Lesser said Mr. Iankovsky originally intended to relocate his family to New York, but their situation has changed and they are no longer planning to move to the city.

Now that he is headed to Alabama, former University of Southern California head football coach Lane Kiffin is unloading real estate in the Los Angeles area.

In December Mr. Kiffin, who was fired by USC in September, listed his 7,300-square-foot house in Manhattan Beach for $6.995 million and a townhouse in El Segundo for $745,000, according to public records. Both properties were listed with Bryn Stroyke of Stroyke Properties. Mr. Stroyke declined to comment on ownership, but said that the townhouse has sold for $737,500 and that the house is now in contract. Mr. Kiffin couldn't be reached for comment.

The story was first reported by Realtor.com.

Corrections & Amplifications In an earlier version of this article, an incorrect photo appeared with an item about a Brunswick, Maine, home where the author Harriet Beecher Stowe is believed to have written "Uncle Tom's Cabin."

Queens Is the New King for Multifamily Investments
By Keiko Morris
838 words
5 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

A New Jersey property investment firm spent $85.25 million last year to buy a 16-story apartment building in Rego Park, Queens. The firm, Treetop Development LLC, is spending another $7 million on upgrades to the building, called Saxon Hall.

The Teaneck, N.J., company, which already had investments in New Jersey and Manhattan, is now negotiating to buy two other properties in Queens.

"For years Queens lagged behind Manhattan and Brooklyn in terms of growth and only now is catching up," said Adam Mermelstein, a principal at Treetop. "We'd like to be part of the upswing."

According to a report to be released today, Treetop has plenty of company in the booming market for apartment properties. Investors spent $1.36 billion to buy multifamily properties in Queens in 2013, more than double the amount in 2012 and almost 3.5 times the amount spent in 2011, according to the report from Ariel Property Advisors, a real-estate investment sales company.

While multifamily property sales in other New York City sectors also rose, the growth in Queens was the steepest outside of Staten Island, which Ariel Property Advisors doesn't track.

Following the migration of renters priced out of Manhattan and parts of Brooklyn, real-estate investors have focused their attention on northwest Queens neighborhoods such as Long Island City, Astoria, Sunnyside, Jackson Heights and Elmhurst.

Many of those areas boast amenities such as restaurants and shops and are within a half-hour train commute to midtown Manhattan, said Michael A. Tortorici, vice president of Ariel.

"There's real value in areas like Astoria and Long Island City with comparable commutes," Mr. Tortorici said. "And they can get all of that for a price that's less expensive."

Developers and investors buying property in Queens last year included newcomers and veterans of the borough, many of them looking for stable and strong income from rental buildings. The Benedict Realty Group LLC, a longtime Queens landlord, purchased 19 rental buildings in Jackson Heights, Elmhurst and Corona for more than $68 million last year.

Overall, the Queens market had 519 sales transactions of investment properties, including multifamily buildings, development sites and commercial properties. Those sales totaled about $2.8 billion, a 46% increase from $1.9 billion in sales in 2012, according to the report.

Sales of development sites -- where many expect multifamily projects to rise -- climbed 59%, to $987 million, in 2013, the report revealed.

While the increases are significant, Mr. Tortorici noted that the sales and property transaction figures for Queens in previous years were low compared with other New York City markets. Manhattan's multifamily property sales south of East 96th Street and West 110th Street reached $6.6 billion in 2013, a 10% increase from the prior year. Brooklyn's multifamily property sales rose 29%, to $1.9 billion, in 2013.

Some real-estate investors in Queens said they turned to the borough because they found Manhattan pricing for properties exorbitant, making it difficult for them to figure out how to make money.

Forest Properties Management Inc., a Massachusetts company that owns and operates rental properties on the East Coast, looked at Williamsburg, Brooklyn, but ended up making its first New York City investment in Astoria.

The company partnered with real-estate investor Jeff Greene and purchased the Exo, a 14-story rental building with a doorman, for $47.2 million. Two-bedroom apartments there range between $2,800 and $3,600.

"Astoria was really interesting to us because pricing for property was a lot better" than markets in Brooklyn and Manhattan, said Anderson Libert, director of acquisitions for Forest Properties. "You can make money out of the box."

Momentum for development and multifamily sales throughout Queens is expected to continue with a number of projects in the pipeline, according to the report from Ariel Property Advisors.

The Related Companies and Sterling Equities are set to begin construction on a $3 billion mixed-use development at Willets Point, near Flushing. And the first phase of construction for Flushing Commons, an $850 million mixed-use project in Flushing, likely will begin this spring.

Unlike new developments in Astoria, the long-delayed Flushing Commons project includes residential condo units, catering to Flushing's large Asian population, which tends to buy rather than rent, said John T. Livingston, CEO of AECOM Capital, the investment arm of AECOM Technology Corp. AECOM Capital joined TDC Development and Construction Corp. and the Rockefeller Group in the project about six months ago.

In addition to being a dense market, Flushing has access to public transportation and isn't far from La Guardia and John F. Kennedy airports. The project will offer residential condo units that will probably be half the price of similar property in Manhattan, Mr. Livingston said.

"Flushing, Queens, is such a hub of commercial and retail activity," Mr. Livingston said. "We're not the first to figure that out."

The Property Report
Deal of the Week: Playing With House Money
By Dawn Wotapka and Kris Hudson
727 words
5 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

HENDERSON, Nev. -- It has been nearly three years since builder Josh Anderson purchased 36 distressed lots just outside of Las Vegas for an average of about $90,000 each, hoping that a bet on troubled land would pay off once the market turned.

If all goes well this week, his gamble could result in a jackpot.

One of the lots Mr. Anderson bought was used to build the "New American Home," the name given to a model home that captures the latest design trends and is unveiled annually at the International Builders' Show. This year's show, a mega convention expected to attract 80,000 members of the home-building and home-renovation industry, opened in Las Vegas on Tuesday.

The home, a sprawling 6,700-square-foot abode with five bedrooms, elevator and rooftop terrace, soon will be listed for sale with a price tag of about $5 million. Mr. Anderson, founder of Element Building Co., hopes that the thousands of people who tour the home -- and the national publicity that ensues -- will help him sell additional lots for between $200,000 and $1 million each.

During the economic downturn, land prices were so depressed that buying then seemed risky. But now "some of these deals are starting to make sense," said Greg Gross, a regional director with Metrostudy. In the case of the New American Home site, "being able to pick that [land] up for that price was a steal."

The lots are carved into the side of a mountain, with views of the desert and strip. Mr. Anderson purchased the land via a short sale after the previous owner, Alpha Land Development, saw the project fall into distress in 2009. "It was pretty much a traumatic thing for all of us," said Leslie Davis, project coordinator of the now-defunct company. "That was pretty much our baby."

Las Vegas was a poster child for the housing bust: Overconstruction and lax lending gave way to plunging home values and rampant foreclosures. Home prices tumbled nearly 60% from the April 2006 peak until the December 2011 trough, according to CoreLogic, a real-estate data and analytics firm.

But like the rest of the nation, the city's real-estate market appears to have hit bottom. The area's median single-family sales price in December was $185,000, up 24% from the prior year, according to the Greater Las Vegas Association of Realtors. Supply is also tight: About 13,000 homes were for sale in December, down nearly 9% from the prior year.

The high-end market, Mr. Anderson's focus, has been particularly brisk. Some 343 homes sold for $1 million or more in Southern Nevada last year, the highest count since 2008.

"When high-end inventory comes on the market, it gets eaten pretty quickly," said Rick Hildreth, a senior adviser with land brokerage Land Advisors Organization. With Las Vegas land prices doubling in the past year, Mr. Hildreth says people who purchased land several years ago now appear savvy.

The New American Home has been a highlight of IBS, one of the nation's largest business trade shows, since the first one was built in Houston in 1984, said Tucker Bernard, a director with the National Association of Home Builders, which hosts IBS. Each year, the NAHB picks a builder for the home and dozens of other suppliers and tradesman donate their time and materials. Participation is considered an honor.

Mr. Anderson paid for any products and labor that weren't donated. He declined to disclose how much he spent, but said that he stood to make a "better-than-average profit."

Things haven't always gone so smoothly. In 2010, while the financial crisis dragged on, Domanico Custom Homes was picked to build the New American Home. It couldn't complete the project due to a lack of capital, and the home fell into foreclosure.

Mr. Anderson said participants remain jittery. "All the suppliers and manufacturers remember 2010 and bring it up," he said, adding that they ask, " 'Are you sure this is going to work?'"

He vowed they have nothing to worry about this year. "There's new capital," he said. "It's going to work."

Property Pulse: Residential Reigns
By Constance Mitchell Ford
87 words
5 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal

C9
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Total construction spending rose 5.3% in December compared with a year earlier to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $930.5 billion.

The biggest driver of the increase in spending was private residential construction, which jumped 18.3% in December.

World
Canada Building Permits Fall Unexpectedly; December Permits Down 4.1% as Planned Construction of Multi-Family Dwellings Slumps
Nirmala Menon
462 words
5 February 2014
05:36
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

OTTAWA—Canadian building permits fell unexpectedly in December as planned construction of multi-family dwellings--including condominiums and apartments--plunged the most in a year, underscoring a cooling in the housing market.

Permits shrank for the second consecutive month, falling 4.1% to 6.48 billion Canadian dollars ($5.85 billion), following a revised 6.6% decline in November, Statistics Canada said Wednesday.

The consensus call was for a 1.5% rebound in December from the originally estimated 6.7% drop the previous month, according to a report from Royal Bank of Canada.

The overall value of building permits for all of 2013 fell 0.1% to C$80.8 billion, the first decline since the 2009 recession when permits shrank 13.3%.

Residential permits were down for a second straight month, falling 9.3% to C$3.70 billion, the lowest level since March.

The decline was driven entirely by multi-family dwellings, which plummeted 21.9%--the largest magnitude since December 2012--to a nine-month low of C$1.47 billion. Most of the decline occurred in British Columbia, Ontario and Alberta, the statistical agency said. Single-family permits rose 1.5% to C$2.23 billion, the third increase in four months.

The strength of the housing market, and particularly the condo sector in major cities, had been a source of concern for policymakers in recent years, prompting the federal government to tighten mortgage insurance rules in mid-2012 for the fourth time in as many years. That led to a slowing in the market, but as bond yields rose last summer on expectations that the U.S. Federal Reserve was about to scale back its bond-buying program, buyers rushed to purchase homes to take advantage of mortgage rates that they had locked in at lower rates. But data since then have shown the market is again slowing.

Despite the cooling, the International Monetary Fund said in a report this week that average house prices are about 10% more than justified by fundamentals, with most of the gap coming from Ontario and Quebec.

Across Canada, municipalities approved construction of 15,565 new residential buildings in December, a decline of 14.2% from November, as the number of multi-family dwellings plunged 21.3%. The number of single-family homes were down 0.1%.

In the non-residential sector, commercial permits plunged 33.5% to C$1.26 billion, mainly from lower planned construction of office buildings in Ontario and recreational facilities and retail stores in British Columbia.

Institutional permits more than doubled to C$939 million, while industrial permits jumped 34.9% to C$576 million.

Vegas Builder Hits the Jackpot; 'New American Home' Will Be Listed for $5 Million
Dawn Wotapka
720 words
4 February 2014

The Wall Street Journal Online

US
Americans Boost Spending on Remodeling; Homeowners Doled Out $130 Billion Last Year for Renovations.
Kris Hudson
925 words
3 February 2014
16:22
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Americans are spending lavishly again to upgrade their homes, indicating they remain confident about the long-term prospects for the recovery despite recent signs of weakness.

Homeowners spent $130 billion on remodeling projects last year, according to data released Monday by the U.S. Census Bureau . That was up 3.1% from 2012 and was the largest amount of home-remodeling spending since 2007, the year that the housing downturn began. Permits for remodeling jobs in the U.S. rose 5.1% last year from 2012, the largest increase since 2010 when the figures began their rebound from a 10-year low, according to permit-tracking company BuildFax.

Howard Bass, a retired museum manager, and Lisette Voyatzis, an attorney, decided to remodel their 2,000-square-foot home in Arlington, Va., rather than endure what they deemed the hassle of buying a new home. The project, which started last June, includes an expansion of their kitchen, the addition of a guest room and bathroom on the first floor and an expansion of the master suite on the second.

The couple purchased the home in 2008 for more than $1 million. Although Mr. Bass says he hasn't checked the home's value since then, real estate website Zillow said the median value of homes in the couple's ZIP Code have increased by 14% in the past two years. The couple landed a home-equity loan to cover roughly a third of the renovation's estimated cost of $400,000 to $500,000.

"Right now, we envision staying in this house for a number of years," Mr. Bass said. "We thought about what we're putting into the house increasing its value. But we're not at all thinking of selling. It just seemed like too much to go through buying and selling."

Rising spending on remodeling is in contrast to recent signs of slowing in new-home construction activity, indicating that a growing number of consumers have decided that upgrading their home might be a better investment than buying a new one. Other factors at play include the limited inventory of existing homes and rising home values, which have made it easier for homeowners to finance their remodeling projects by borrowing against their equity.

Home-equity lending—which sank to its lowest level of the past 10 years in 2010—jumped 18% last year to $123.4 billion, according to estimates by Moody's Analytics. That increased lending stems mostly from rising home values, which created more equity for many homeowners and lifted some who previously owed more than their home was worth, into positive territory. According to real estate data firm CoreLogic , two-thirds of all U.S. homeowners had at least 20% equity in their homes as of last year's third quarter, up from 53.2% two years earlier.

"If home prices are going up and people have more equity in their home, things like remodeling and refurbishment will do well, because it's effectively the way of playing the reinvestment game," said Joseph LaVorgna , chief U.S. economist for Deutsche Bank AG .

The increased remodeling activity comes as thousands of contractors and tradesmen are expected to convene in Las Vegas this week at two trade shows geared to the remodeling and home-building industry, the International Builder Show and the Kitchen and Bath Industry Show. The combined attendance is expected to exceed 80,000, the highest since 2008.

"From 2010 to 2013 is the difference between night and day," said Gary Drake, chief executive of Drake General Contractor Inc., a Los Angeles remodeling company that says it posted a 30% increase in revenue last year to $4 million. "The phone is ringing more. Old clients are calling me. Architects are offering me work. I'm actually turning down work now."

The increase in remodeling projects has revved up sales for a variety of home-improvement companies, from retailers such as Lowe's Cos . to paint company Sherwin-Williams Co ., and local contractors who install everything from granite countertops to carpeting.

Michael Grosswendt, a builder and renovator of high-end homes in the Los Angeles area, said revenue and staff at his company, All Coast Construction, were nearly halved during the downturn to as low as $10 million and 25 workers.

As the market recovered in 2013, Mr. Grosswendt's revenue climbed to $15 million. These days he's turning down work while keeping his staff at 25 and spending more time on fewer projects.

Suppliers are cashing in, too. Masco Corporation , owner of brands including KraftMaid cabinets, Behr paints and Delta faucets, posted $2.2 billion in sales in the third quarter, up 12% from a year earlier partly on strong sales of windows and cabinets. Residential remodeling projects typically account for roughly three-quarters of Masco 's sales.

Mohawk Industries , a global manufacturer of tile, carpet and flooring, saw U.S. sales of its product lines decline by 30% to 40% during the downturn. In 2013, Mohawk posted quarterly gains, such as a 12% increase in sales of ceramic tile in the U.S. and a 3% boost in carpet sales, due at least in part to more remodeling.

"Those year-over-year gains for our products for remodeling are the first we have seen in the past six years," said Frank Boykin , Mohawk's chief financial officer. "This is an indication of the potential pent-up demand in the remodel market."

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Downtown Manhattan Penthouse Sells for $50.9 Million; Half-moon home lists for $11.75 million; Southern California home goes on the market home for $25 million
Candace Taylor
1264 words
30 January 2014
15:01
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

A penthouse in Manhattan's Chelsea neighborhood has closed for $50.9 million, setting a record for the most expensive home sale downtown, according to listing agent Vickey Barron of Douglas Elliman.

Called Penthouse One, the 5,955-square-foot, five-bedroom, 5½-bath apartment takes up the 24th floor of Walker Tower, an Art Deco office building converted to condos by JDS Development Group and Property Markets Group. It has a private south-facing terrace and floor-to-ceiling windows on all four sides, allowing views of the Statue of Liberty, the Chrysler Building and the Empire State Building, Ms. Barron said. The unit also has nearly 13-foot-high coffered ceilings and three wood-burning fireplaces.

The apartment was listed in September for $55 million, and went into contract less than a month later, said Ms. Barron, who represented the buyer and seller. The all-cash deal closed last week, said Elliott Joseph, a principal at Property Markets Group. Mr. Joseph and Ms. Barron said they couldn't disclose the identity of the buyer, but Ms. Barron said the purchaser "was in the penthouse less than five minutes and said, 'I'll take it.'" According to records, the largest prior sale south of 34th street in Manhattan was a penthouse at 18 Gramercy Park South that sold for $42 million in August 2013.

Walker Tower was designed by architect Ralph Walker and completed in 1929 as an office for New York Telephone. The developers bought the building in 2009 from Verizon for $25.25 million, according to public records, and conducted an extensive renovation. The only other unit available in the 47-unit building is Penthouse Two, on the 23rd floor, which is listed for $47.5 million, Mr. Joseph said.

At 6,515 square feet, Penthouse Two is larger than Penthouse One, but the top floor fetches a premium, Mr. Joseph said. Still, he said if the market stays strong, when Penthouse Two sells "we might break our own record."

Living in "the Crescent House," a contemporary home by noted architectural designer Wallace Cunningham, is "kind of an adventure," said owner Esther Fischer, who has listed the Encinitas, Calif., house for $11.75 million.

Mr. Cunningham designed the waterfront home in 2000 for Ms. Fischer and her late husband, San Diego real-estate developer Arnold "Bud" Fischer. Made of concrete, steel and glass, the 6,500-square-foot home has four bedrooms, and four full and two half bathrooms.

When visitors enter the home, the first thing they see is an infinity pool shaped like a crescent moon—hence the home's name, Ms. Fischer said.

The pool wraps around a circular terrace, which is accessed by a curved ramp, Mr. Cunningham said.

The home's design was inspired by the moon and the views from the home's perch on a sandstone bluff some 80 feet above the ocean, Mr. Cunningham said.

Much of the ocean-facing side of the house, he said, is floor-to-ceiling glass intended to maximize light and water views. In fact, Mr. Cunningham said he "couldn't bear" to build a wall in the living room, where Ms. Fischer wanted to hang a painting by contemporary artist Squeak Carnwath. Instead, they suspended the piece on cables in front of the glass.

The home also has terraces off the living room, office and master bedroom. There is an elevator, a two-car garage and a gym.

The Fischers moved into the house in 2003. Mr. Fischer died last year, and his wife said she is selling because the property is now "a lot of house for me." Still, "it makes me very sad to leave."

The listing agent is Douglas Harwood of Coldwell Banker.

Joe Harper's job as chief executive of California's Del Mar racetrack involves lots of entertaining: The summer racing season is "a seven-week long party," Mr. Harper said.

So when he and his wife, Barbara, set out to build their house in the so-called Del Mar Beach Colony, about 20 miles north of San Diego, they made sure there was plenty of space for parties. The 4,700-square-foot, five-bedroom, five-bathroom oceanfront home, now on the market for $25 million with Brian Pasco of Coldwell Banker, can accommodate some 200 guests, Ms. Harper said—especially when there is a dance floor set up on the beach.

The house is on about 60 feet of waterfront, Mr. Harper said. It has a balcony on the second floor and five wood-burning fireplaces, including one in an outdoor seating area. There is also an attached studio apartment.

Bing Crosby founded the Del Mar racetrack in 1934, and since then it has been known as "a playground of Hollywood," said Mr. Harper. For years, TV personalities Merv Griffin and Eva Gabor were visitors at his home, he said. Mr. Harper's grandfather was filmmaker Cecil B. DeMille, and as a child Mr. Harper appeared in several movies, including as a slave in "the Ten Commandments."

The Del Mar Beach Colony developed in the 1930s as a spot for celebrities to buy or rent homes during the racing season, Mr. Harper said. The Harpers bought their home in 1970 for about $170,000, he said. The Harpers initially used an existing bungalow as a summer home. But in the 1980s they removed the house and built the current structure.

The couple is selling because Mr. Harper, 70, is "backing off a little bit" from his responsibilities at the racetrack, though he said he isn't quite ready for retirement. The couple wants to spend more time at their Arizona ranch, he said.

The singer-songwriter had listed the two-bedroom, 2 ½-bathroom Soho apartment for $3.895 million in October with Brown Harris Stevens. According to the records, Mr. Mayer purchased the condo for $2.17 million in 2005. The sale was previously reported by the Real Deal.

Gold medal-winning figure skater Evan Lysacek bought a two-bedroom condo in Chicago for $850,000. The 1,600-square-foot unit is on the 45th floor of a high-rise just off North Michigan Avenue, according to Julie Busby of Belgravia Realty Group, who represented Mr. Lysacek. The purchase included a deeded indoor parking spot. Mr. Lysacek won a gold medal in the 2010 Olympics. He is not competing in the 2014 winter Olympics in Sochi due to injury. The deal was previously reported by the Chicago Tribune.

In a deal expected to close for more than $90 million, the government of Qatar is in contract to buy a Manhattan townhouse on East 64th Street to use as a New York consulate. The Corcoran Group's Carrie Chiang, who represented the seller, said when the deal closes in April, it will set a record for the most expensive townhouse sold in Manhattan. U.S. Housing Market Hits Headwinds; Pending Sales Slide as Cold Weather, Rising Prices Dent Demand
Nick Timiraos
944 words
30 January 2014
09:44
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The housing market, already showing signs of slowing after a big jump in mortgage rates last summer, cooled further at the end of last year, according to economic reports and the latest quarterly results from several big home builders.

An index measuring contracts to buy existing homes fell 8.7% in December from November to its lowest level in more than two years, according to the National Association of Realtors. Pending sales stood 8.8% below the year-earlier level.

Meanwhile, housing construction's contribution to gross domestic product, the broadest measure of goods and services produced across the economy, declined during the fourth quarter, subtracting one-third of a percentage point from economic growth. That was the first such decline in more than three years, according to the Commerce Department report.

Some economists blamed the dip in sales and construction on cold winter weather. "The drop was inconsistent with the fundamentals, and you'll see a snapback in the current quarter," said Joseph LaVorgna, chief U.S. economist at Deutsche Bank.

But others aren't so sure and are questioning whether the recent pullback will be temporary or the beginning of slower growth. "Weather was a contributing factor but not the only thing here, especially in the West," where contract activity dropped 16% from a year earlier, said Stan Humphries, chief economist at Zillow Inc.

Most observers agree that one big factor behind the slowdown in housing activity is rising prices, which were up nearly 24% in November from March 2012, when the market bottomed, according to the Standard & Poor's/Case-Shiller home-price index. The price gains have been fueled largely by low supplies of homes for sale.

Record low interest rates two years ago also unleashed pent-up demand from traditional buyers, and many found themselves in competition with investors who had been actively buying up cheap foreclosures. The frenzy appeared to subside last summer, however, after mortgage rates jumped by nearly a full percentage point, to around 4.5% for a 30-year fixed-rate loan in June, from 3.5% in May.

Some real-estate agents have blamed the sales slowdown on inventory shortages. "You could sell more if there was more product available," said Donna Evers, chief executive of real-estate brokerage Evers & Co. in Washington, D.C.

But sales have slowed recently even in markets where inventories are now rising. Housing markets in coastal California, where prices have posted double-digit gains over the past year, "have gotten out over their skis," said Mr. Humphries. "It's not that [buyers] can't find homes to buy. It's that they don't want to buy them at the prices being offered," he said.

A related concern is that consumers' balance sheets are too weak, and that the problem will only worsen as interest rates and prices climb. Given the slow pace of wage growth, "it is difficult to find sustainable support for the rapid growth in home prices across much of the country," said Stefan Hilts, a director at Fitch Ratings in New York, in a report released Thursday. Mr. Hilts said that home-price growth has been "excessive."

"More jobs, and ideally better-paying jobs, will be critical in driving housing demand to peak levels," said Richard Dugas Jr., chief executive of home builder PulteGroup Inc., on an earnings call Thursday.

There's also the related question of why new-home construction hasn't ramped up faster. Labor, land, and supply costs have also been harder to manage as builders rebuild their supply chains, and it has been difficult for small builders to obtain loans. As a result, builders have focused on getting higher prices rather than higher sales volumes, said Brad Hunter, chief economist for Metrostudy, a division of Hanley Wood LLC that tracks U.S. home construction.

The focus on building more expensive homes pushed the average price of newly built homes to an all-time high of $320,900 last year, up 9.8% from 2012, according to the U.S. Census Bureau. Last year's figure exceeded the previous high of $313,600 in 2007, though sales last year were nearly half of what they were in 2007.

Those heady price increases showed up in the fourth-quarter results reported by three national builders on Thursday. Pulte, Beazer Homes USA Inc. and Ryland Group Inc. posted big revenue gains for the quarter due mostly to price increases, but those gains came at the expense of sales volumes. The number of new orders for Pulte declined 18% in the fourth quarter from a year earlier. Beazer's fell by 4% Ryland's, by 4.4%.

Several economists expect construction to pick up this year as builders shift to focusing more on production volume now that price increases largely have played out. The National Association of Home Builders predicts housing starts will increase roughly 24.1% this year to 1.15 million units, while Barclays economists estimate 1.25 million units.

For now, move-up buyers are representing an increasingly larger portion of a much-reduced market for new homes. D.R. Horton Inc., the largest U.S. builder by number of home sales, reported Tuesday that first-time buyers accounted for 41% of the mortgages it originated in its quarter ended Dec. 31, down from 50% a year earlier. Move-up buyers generally have the financial wherewithal to qualify for mortgages under stricter guidelines, and the rising equity in their homes last year gave them firepower to consider buying new ones.

Total revenues rose 5.6% to $1.66 billion.

Kwanwoo Jun

28 January 2014

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

SEOUL—South Korea's industrial output grew surprisingly strongly last month, reflecting robust demand from the U.S. and other advanced economies as well as China, Korea's largest export market.

South Korea's factory output increased 3.4% in December from the previous month—the biggest gain since June 2009—blowing past an expected rise of 0.5% in a Wall Street Journal poll of economists.

Other data released Wednesday supported the upbeat picture of the Korean economy, a key barometer of global trade and growth.

The nation's current account, the broadest measure of trade with the rest of the world, grew to a surplus of $6.43 billion in December from $6.03 billion a month earlier. For the whole of 2013, South Korea posted a record $70.73 billion surplus.

Economists said the latest set of figures give further support to the view that South Korea's key export markets are on a sustained recovery track. Exports account for just under half of South Korea's economy.

"The main factor behind the large [current account] surplus remains the strength in exports, as the firming global backdrop continues to support demand for Korean goods," said Barclays economist Wai Ho Leong.

"As demand in the U.S. and Europe continues to firm, we expect Korean exports to continue to perform well over the coming year," he said.

The U.S. economy is in its firmest period of recovery since the 2008 global financial crisis. The U.S. jobless rate has fallen to 6.7% as of December compared with a peak of 10% in 2009.

The European economy is also picking up, with the United Kingdom economy expected to have grown 1.9% in 2013, according to its statistics agency.

Particularly important to South Korea is the health of the Chinese economy, which absorbs a quarter of total shipments from South Korea. Concerns about China were increased by a recent survey that pointed toward a slow in activity in China's factory sector for the first time in six months in January.

Strong factory production in South Korea is also an encouraging sign for Chinese growth because Korea increasingly ships parts for assembly in China.

Trade data for December, released earlier this month, showed South Korean exports to the U.S. rose 13.2% from a year earlier, while shipments to the EU gained 1.8%. South Korean exports to China increased 8.3% on the year in December.

By product, South Korean shipments of memory chips increased by 20.1% on the year in December because of growing demand from the U.S., Bank of Korea data showed. Exports of cars also surged 14.5% last month on rising demand, especially from the U.S., the Middle East and the European Union. Shipments of machinery jumped 18.4%.

South Korea is home to global manufacturers of vehicles, ships, mobile handsets, semiconductors, petrochemicals and electronics goods shipped to various markets of the world.

Nomura economist Kwon Young-sun said the robust output and current account data in December would likely lead South Korea's growth momentum into the first quarter of 2014, also helped by signs of a property market recovery.

"We expect pent-up demand to materialize further, supported by government measures to increase social welfare spending and boost the property market."

For the whole of 2013, the South Korean economy expanded 2.8%, in a pickup from a three-year low of 2% growth in 2012.

The BOK has said the economy will continue to pick up through this year, helped by an international recovery, forecasting growth of 3.8% for 2014 and 4% for 2015.

By Jonathan House
569 words
28 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal

English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

New-home sales ended 2013 on a weak note amid unusually cold weather, renewing concerns about the housing industry's rebound.

Sales of newly built homes fell 7% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 414,000 in December, from 445,000 in November, the Commerce Department said Monday. November's figure was revised down by 19,000.

December sales came in below the 455,000 median annual pace forecast by 15 analysts surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires and were at their lowest level since the summer, when rising mortgage rates undermined demand. Rates jumped in response to Federal Reserve plans to start dialing back a bond-buying program designed to keep borrowing costs low. Home sales dipped to a 373,000 annual pace in July but then recovered in the following months.

The final figures for 2013 showed housing sales running at less than half the pace of the years even before the housing boom last decade led to a surge in construction.

Monday's report was the latest in a string of disappointing releases economists attribute to last month's cold and stormy weather.

The housing report "will keep alive the debate over whether the recovery in home sales will get back on track soon," said Jim O'Sullivan, economist at High Frequency Economics.

Surging demand for housing, prompted by steady job gains and historically low interest rates, has been an important driver of the economic recovery. But new-home building has run ahead of sales in recent months. "The sales pace will need to pick back up in coming months," said Ted Wieseman, an economist at Morgan Stanley.

Economists expect interest rates to rise this year as the economic recovery strengthens and the Fed tries to wind down its bond-buying program. Fed officials are expected to announce another reduction in the purchases to $65 billion a month from the current $75 billion after their two-day meeting on Tuesday and Wednesday.

The average interest rate on a 30-year, fixed-rate mortgage was 4.46% in December, according to Freddie Mac. That was almost a percentage point higher than rates in early May.

The median sale price for a new home in December was $270,200, up 4.6% from a year earlier and the highest level since April. That is a sign of strong demand for housing but is also another factor undermining affordability in many of the nation's hottest markets.

Some recent data have shown prospective buyers adapting to higher prices and mortgage rates. A gauge of pending sales of existing homes, which make up the bulk of transactions, showed an uptick after falling off in the middle part of the year. New-home construction also has picked up after a summer lull.

The National Association of Realtors last week said 5.09 million existing homes were sold in 2013. That was up 9% from 2012 and the best annual performance since 2006.

The trade group forecast that existing-home sales will be at about the same level this year. But it said the stock of new homes needs to increase to ease supply shortages and temper rapid price gains. Many economists predict that strong demand for housing will prompt an acceleration in home-building activity in the year ahead.

U.K. Economic Recovery Slows Slightly in Fourth Quarter; Growth Is Widely Expected to Accelerate in 2014
Alex Brittain

28 January 2014

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LONDON—Britain's economic recovery ebbed in the final three months of 2013, a slight slowdown that could strengthen policy makers' resolve to keep stimulus flowing to encourage further growth.

The Office for National Statistics said Tuesday output in the economy grew 0.7% between October and December, down from a 0.8% expansion rate in the two previous quarters.

Growth in annualized terms in the fourth quarter was 2.8%.

A fall in construction output in November was largely to blame for the slowdown, the statistics office said, estimating output in that sector fell by 0.3% during the quarter overall. Other sectors of the economy grew steadily.

The ONS didn't make any estimate for the likely impact of flooding that hit parts of the U.K. at the end of December.

Sterling fell against both the dollar and the euro in the minutes after the data were released, before recovering. Prices of U.K. government bonds edged lower.

The data matched economists' forecasts in a Wall Street Journal poll.

Growth is widely expected to accelerate in 2014. The International Monetary Fund earlier this month predicted growth of 2.4% over the year.

The economy grew 1.9% in 2013 as a whole, its fastest rate of growth since 2007 and likely one of the best performances among developed nations. Other countries have yet to release growth figures for the year.

The economy began a strong recovery at the start of 2013 after sputtering since 2010, when the coalition government took power with a policy of fiscal consolidation meant to address the huge debts the U.K. had racked up, partly as a consequence of the financial crisis.

George Osborne, the U.K.'s finance chief, welcomed the ONS figures but said the government cannot back away from its plan to curb benefits and other forms of government spending, while cutting taxes for businesses.

"It is more evidence that our long-term economic plan is working," Mr. Osborne said in a statement. "But the job is not done, and it is clear that the biggest risk now to the recovery would be abandoning the plan that's delivering jobs and a brighter economic future."

Britons will go to the polls in the spring of 2015. The opposition Labour Party says policy should change to help boost workers' incomes.

"We have now seen four successive quarters of significant growth and the economy does seem to be improving more consistently," Joe Grice, the statistics office's chief economic adviser, said of Tuesday's data.

U.K. economic output has yet to recover fully from a steep recession in 2009, however, remaining 1.3% below the precrisis peak.

The fourth-quarter growth rate failed to match Bank of England officials' predictions for the economy to accelerate further during the period.

That fact is likely to underpin policy makers' commitment to keep monetary policy loose, despite the recovery seen to date and widespread predictions of further growth.

Officials on the BOE's Monetary Policy Committee have said they won't consider tightening policy even after the U.K.'s unemployment rate falls below a 7% threshold they established in the summer.

The jobless rate fell to 7.1% in the three months through November, the latest figures show.

The fourth-quarter growth disappointment "may help to quell murmurs of some support among committee members for an interest-rate rise sooner rather than later," said Martin Beck, economist at Capital Economics, a consultancy.

Still, conditions are in place for stronger growth in gross domestic product next year, he said. "With rising real incomes set to provide stronger support for household spending and investment set to pick up, we expect GDP to expand by a healthy 3% in 2014."
