WASHINGTON—Some regions of the U.S. are confronting labor shortages in construction and other high-skill fields, according to the Federal Reserve's survey of regional economic conditions released Wednesday.

The Dallas Fed district reported "acute labor shortages" for auditors, engineers, truck drivers and construction workers in late November and December.

The Cleveland Fed said hiring was "sluggish" for most industries, but construction firms were hiring. "Builders reported a scarcity of high-skilled trade workers," according to the report. "As a result, there is upward pressure on wages, and subcontractors are demanding and getting higher rates."

The latest beige book, which describes economic conditions across the central bank's 12 districts, painted a picture of firming labor markets across the U.S. as economic growth accelerates and major sectors such as real estate and manufacturing recover from the housing downturn and recession.

"The labor markets showed signs of tightening," the Minneapolis district reported, with 30% of businesses saying they expect to hire more full-time workers in 2014 versus 18% who expect to have fewer full-time employees.

In the Richmond district, there were "numerous reports of strong labor demand," though the report also said few businesses offered permanent jobs to seasonal workers and there was high turnover among low-skill workers.

In all, two-thirds of districts reported "small to moderate" increases in hiring, according to the report, and many companies were optimistic as 2014 began. In the New York district, most companies said they kept staffing flat as 2013 came to a close, but "substantially more businesses plan to expand than reduce their workforces in 2014."

The bright spots in Wednesday's survey came as hiring slowed across the U.S. last month, according to the Labor Department, which last week estimated the economy nationwide added just 74,000 nonfarm jobs in December. The unemployment rate dropped to 6.7%, largely because people left the workforce.

The beige book said overall economic growth was "moderate" in nine regions and "modest" in two others in late November and December. The Kansas City district said conditions "held steady" in December.

The last such survey, released in December, showed seven districts growing moderately and four districts growing modestly. One other district only said economic activity was expanding.

In Fed reports, the word "modest" has been used at times to indicate an expansion that's slightly slower than "moderate" growth.

The beige book was released two weeks before the Fed's policy committee is scheduled to meet Jan. 28-29. The Fed must decide at the meeting how to proceed with winding down its bond-buying program, which seeks to boost the economy by lowering borrowing costs.

The Fed announced in December it would reduce its $85 billion a month in bond purchases by $10 billion, starting this month. Outgoing Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke said the central bank is likely to continue trimming its bond buying this year if economic data "broadly support" policy makers' expectations for improving employment and low inflation.

The Fed survey found retail spending was "modestly to moderately higher" in most areas compared with a year earlier. But the Richmond district reported slowing retail spending and the Kansas City district said holiday sales were lower than expected, "which retailers there attributed to a shorter selling season and harsh weather conditions."

Most areas reported improving real-estate markets, with residential sales, prices and construction on the rise. Two-thirds of districts said commercial property sales and leasing were up, too.

Prices were described as "stable" in about half the districts and most of the rest reported "small increases," with a couple exceptions. A frozen fish producer in the Boston district, for instance, said prices for shrimp and haddock were "through the roof."

Eight of the 12 districts reported "small to moderate" increases in wages.

While spending on tourism and leisure was reportedly "mixed" across the country, the manufacturing sector saw "steady growth" and steady employment.

"A manufacturer in the Dallas district said that for the first time since before the recession, his firm had too many jobs to bid on," according to the report.

No major changes in bank lending volume were reported, though six districts reported "slight to moderate growth," three saw no change and one— New York—saw a "moderate decline in loan volume."

Energy production and demand also remained strong, according to the report. The Atlanta district reported an increase in deep water oil exploration in the Gulf of Mexico, "bolstered by the completion of recent pipeline infrastructure projects."

TORONTO—Canadian existing-home sales fell 1.8% in December from November as real-estate activity in most major cities slowed at the end of the year, the country's real-estate trade group said in its monthly report Wednesday.

Compared with a year earlier, home sales were up 12.9% in December, the Canadian Real Estate Association said. Home sales were up just 0.8% in all of 2013 compared with the prior year, CREA said.

The Multiple Listing Home Price Index, which is weighted to compensate for fluctuations in the composition of sales, rose 4.3% year-over-year in December, CREA said.

CREA said the number of newly listed homes was down 4.3% month-over-month in December.

Canadian home sales softened after the Canadian government tightened mortgage rules in the summer of 2012, and recovered to hit a high in September 2013.

Although sales activity isn't expected to reach the kind of highs seen in September 2013, this year could still be a strong one for Canadian sales, CREA said.

"Absent further mortgage rule changes, sales in 2014 may surpass the annual total for 2013 if demand holds steady near current levels as strengthening economic and better job growth offset the impact of further expected marginal mortgage interest rate increases," Gregory Klump, chief economist for CREA, said in a statement.

The average, the non-seasonally adjusted home price in Canada rose 10.4% year-over-year in December, CREA said, to 389,119 Canadian dollars ($355,035).

CREA said the price gain reflects strong sales activity in Vancouver and Toronto, two of Canada's most active and expensive markets. Omitting both cities would result in a year-over-year price increase of just 4.6%.

HONG KONG—Hong Kong's leader Wednesday laid out an ambitious agenda to boost housing supply and narrow the split between the rich and poor, as public frustration over a lack of social mobility intensifies.

In his second policy address, Chief Executive Leung Chun-ying said the densely populated city plans to permit construction of taller residential buildings and develop a new metropolis on the island of Lantau.

The city of more than seven million has experienced a record property boom in recent years, with prices more than doubling since 2008, ranking among the highest in the world. Mr. Leung said he was concerned that such a phenomenon, as well as cramped living spaces, is discouraging young families from having children.

"I often think, if a kindergartner asks me, 'Where will I live when I grow up?,' what answer should I give? 'Nowhere' is definitely not an acceptable answer," said Mr. Leung, noting the government has also identified 150 hectares of space that could be rezoned for residential use, enough to provide 210,000 additional apartments.

The stretched housing market has also helped fuel poverty in the city, which Mr. Leung plans to combat with a 3 billion Hong Kong dollar (US$387 million) program to increase subsidies to working low-income families numbering around 710,000 people.

The government has been concerned about the persistence of intergenerational poverty in Hong Kong, traditionally known for its numerous mainland Chinese immigrants who have bootstrapped their way into enormous wealth. Class mobility appears to have become more stagnant over the years, according to the city's no. 2 official, Carrie Lam.

The launch of the program to boost subsidies is partly the result of a "philosophical" shift within the government, said Ms. Lam, as the benefits of economic growth have failed to be evenly distributed, and that even working families remain in poverty.

Mr. Leung's populist emphasis on land supply and poverty alleviation came as the city is also wrestling with greater public demands for democracy. Over the new year, thousands marched to demand universal suffrage before 2017. Beijing has pledged that by that year at the earliest the former British colony will be able to directly elect its leader.

In July, some 66,000 took to the streets, according to police estimates, with marchers complaining that the administration hasn't moved aggressively enough to pave the way for democratic elections, a topic that Mr. Leung mentioned only briefly in his Wednesday address.

As part of new plans to boost housing supply, the chief executive said the government will relax existing building restrictions outside the most densely populated urban centers to allow residential buildings to be as much as 20% taller.

Mr. Leung—who has made affordable housing one of his signature issues since taking office in 2012—also said the government will develop eastern Lantau, an island known for its green space and as the site of Disneyland, into a central business district to allow it to better tap into the growth of the surrounding Pearl River Delta, as well as to accommodate new residential projects.

Combined with other property measures, Mr. Leung said the city was due for a significant increase in housing supply in the coming years. He estimates that the amount of housing produced by the private sector will rise by about 40% over the next five years, as compared with the previous five.

Analysts said the measures, in addition to stiff property taxes Mr. Leung has levied on purchases since assuming office, are likely to prompt residential prices in the city to decline by as much as 15% in the coming years.

"The property price increases that were driven by a constraint in supply will reverse, and Hong Kong's residential price correction will become a multiyear trend," said CLSA analyst Nicole Wong, who expects residential prices will fall as much as 15% in the next 24 months.

In his remarks, Mr. Leung said the city will continue to develop its tourism, one of its pillar industries, though he acknowledged that the crush of tourists—some 35 million from mainland China alone arrived in the territory during 2012—can be taxing. Hong Kong will continue to focus on more infrastructure to accommodate such visitors, with a particular emphasis on attracting high-spending ones, he said. Government officials said Wednesday one way such a policy could be carried out is through the construction of more luxury hotels.

Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The U.K. housing market is never short of a naysayer.

But the country's largest house builder by volumes, Barratt Developments, saw off its critics again Tuesday reporting a 71% surge in forward sales in the second half of last year. For house builders, there are reasons to think the recovery is still off to the races.

True, house builder stocks got out of the gate well before the property market started picking up speed, rising by more than 160% over the past two years. The sector now trades on 1.8 times forecast 2014 book value compared with an average of just over two times at historic cyclical peaks, based on Jefferies estimates.

But builders have ample scope to increase earnings as the recovery gathers momentum. Government-backed programs like Help to Buy, which make mortgages available to buyers with small deposits, have kick-started bank lending, stimulating demand for homes. New mortgage approvals rose by almost a fifth year-over-year in the first 11 months of 2013 but they are still roughly half 2006's peak, according to Bank of England data. Assuming mortgage lending keeps growing, that's a bullish sign for housing-market transactions, which tend to lag mortgage approvals by a few months.

Meanwhile, house builders are just at the beginning of a run of projects on land bought cheaply over the past four years. Barratt acquired more than 11,000 plots in the second half of last year—22% more than in the same period of 2012. Those were bought at prices on which Barratt expects to make at least 25% returns on capital employed. And although builders can't avoid the prospect of rising costs, particularly labor, selling prices are increasing too: Barratt's rose 13.7% in the last six months of 2013.

Besides, even if land values start to rise across the U.K. again, Barratt could always return more capital to shareholders. Suspending land purchases for a year would still leave it with 3½ years' supply and free up £700 million ($1.15 billion) in cash flow to fund a special dividend, notes Credit Suisse. That's equivalent to just under a fifth of its market capitalization.

With Barratt trading on 1.46 times forecast 2014 book value based on Jefferies estimates, a discount to peers, investors may be champing at the bit for a while yet.

SYDNEY—The Australian dollar is trading close to a one-month high against the U.S. currency, as traders eased bets the country's central bank will continue lowering interest rates in 2014.

The Aussie has risen in recent days as a raft of domestic data pointed to a strengthening economic recovery, seen as reducing the need for further monetary easing that would exert downward pressure on the local currency.

What's more, any further rate cuts would risk fueling more gains in house prices—and the central bank has already expressed concern that the housing market, if it continues to rise rapidly, may start overheating.

As recently as December, investors were betting on a 50% chance of one rate cut this year, according to overnight index swaps. The odds have now dropped to about 25%. A few bets are even being placed on a rate increase in 2014, something almost unthinkable last year while data was still mostly patchy.

"The market is increasingly pricing out the chances of further rate cuts," said David Forrester, a Singapore-based currency strategist at Macquarie Bank.

International factors have also contributed to the Aussie's gains since Friday, particularly the greenback's sharp drop against both the Aussie and yen following the release of weak U.S. employment data last week.

The Australian dollar traded above parity for much of the past three years, but fell sharply in 2013 to as low as US$0.8000 as speculation grew that the U.S. Federal Reserve would begin tapering its bond-buying program. The gains over the past few days have taken it back above the US$0.9000 level.

The resumption of the currency's upward trajectory is a concern for the central bank, which has been trying to talk the Aussie down to try and assist the country's struggling exporters, manufacturers in particular.

The central bank's eight rate cuts over the past two years have been aimed at spurring nonmining parts of the economy as a long resources boom cools and pushes up unemployment.

Australia's economy is closely tied to the fortunes of China, its biggest trading partner, whose slowdown in 2012 set in motion a parallel downturn in the economy from which it is still struggling to recover—even with interest rates at a record low 2.5%.

Still, some economic data—including retail, housing and sentiment measures—are beginning to look more promising, raising confidence a corner may have been turned.

Some retailers reported record sales over the Christmas period, while a nongovernment survey this month showed new-home sales rose at the fastest pace in almost four years in November, boding well for a pickup in areas such as construction and high-street spending.

In many investors' minds, all of that is diminishing the chances of the central bank's rate-cutting cycle continuing this year, in turn making the Australian dollar look more appealing.

"The data has surely been a factor" in the Aussie's recent gains, said Sean Callow, a Sydney-based currency strategist at Westpac Banking Corp.

At the same time, the central bank is worried by the pace of gains in house prices, which in major cities rose almost 10% last year as low rates encouraged property investment.

Its concern is that investment housing, rather than buyers looking to move in, is driving the increases, creating the potential risk of a housing crash that would quickly unwind improvements elsewhere in the economy.

With that in mind, currency traders are seeing the chances of central bank rate cuts this year rapidly diminishing.

Late in Asia on Tuesday, the Australian dollar was trading at US$0.9035. It traded as high as US$0.9088 in New York on Monday, its highest level since Dec. 11.

English

LONDON—The annual rate of inflation in the U.K. fell in December to its lowest level in more than four years, a surprise decline that should reinforce the Bank of England's commitment to keep interest rates low to nurture the recovery.

The Office for National Statistics said Tuesday that annual inflation in the U.K. fell to the central bank's target of 2% in December from 2.1% in November, the lowest rate since Nov. 2009.

The ONS said the fall was driven by a smaller increase than a year earlier in prices for food and beverages, which helped offset bigger gains in prices for household gas and electricity.

Economists had expected annual inflation to rise to 2.2% in December following price increases in domestic energy bills announced by major suppliers in late 2013.

The slowdown should bolster the BOE's determination to keep borrowing costs for households and businesses down to support a strengthening economic rebound.

Central-bank officials led by Gov. Mark Carney have pledged to keep the U.K.'s benchmark interest rate at a low of 0.5% as long as inflation is forecast to remain close to the central bank's 2% target. Officials have said they wish to see unemployment drop to 7% before they consider tightening policy. The jobless rate was 7.4% in October.

Economists said Tuesday the fall in inflation should enable officials to keep their benchmark rate stable even after that jobless threshold is met.

"Unemployment is fast approaching the BOE's 7% threshold for considering a rate hike, but the lack of inflation pressure gives them room to delay a first hike until next year," said Robert Wood, chief U.K. economist at Berenberg Bank.

The fall in U.K. inflation will help ease a squeeze on Britons' incomes, which have suffered in recent years as wage rises have failed into keep pace with price increases. A fall in British living standards is set to be an important battleground between Prime Minister David Cameron's governing coalition and the opposition Labour Party in a general election scheduled for 2015.

The slowdown in U.K. inflation echoes similar declines in many advanced economies, including the U.S. and euro zone, a reflection of subdued demand and weak commodity prices world-wide.

Further data Tuesday showed other inflationary pressures in the U.K. economy remain contained. Prices charged by companies for their goods at the factory gate rose 1% on the year in December, while the prices firms pay for raw materials fell 1.2%.

One area where prices are rising quickly is in the property market. ONS data Friday showed house prices were 5.4% higher in November than a year earlier, a slightly smaller gain than in October, when prices rose 5.5% on the year.

The rise in house prices has sparked fears the U.K. could be at risk of developing a dangerous property bubble.

BOE officials say they are monitoring the housing market closely and will act to rein in excessively risky lending, perhaps by slapping limits on how much home buyers can borrow or raising bank capital requirements. Raising the BOE's benchmark interest rate will be the last line of defense against any bubble, Mr. Carney has said.

Industrial production across the 17 countries that shared the euro in November rose at the fastest pace in 3½ years, an indication that the currency area's economy likely grew for the third straight quarter as 2013 drew to a close.

The surprisingly strong rise in output from the euro zone's factories removes some doubts about the sustainability of the economic recovery. While business surveys had been positive during the three months through December, hard data had been weak, with industrial production, construction output and retail sales having fallen in October.

Figures released last week, however, showed retail sales rose at the fastest pace on record in November, and the jump in industrial production suggests it was a strong month for the euro zone's economy.

The European Central Bank expects to see a "gradual" recovery in the euro-zone economy this year, and therefore doesn't expect the inflation rate to weaken much below the 0.8% recorded in December, although it has pledged to act "decisively" if that were to occur.

He echoed ECB President Mario Draghi's assertion that there is no prospect for a period of deflation over either the short or medium term, and said there was no immediate need for the ECB to take action to boost growth and inflation.

"The modest recovery in growth…in the next few months…supports our view that the ECB is likely to keep its monetary policy stance unchanged," said Violante di Canossa, an economist at Credit Suisse.

The European Union's statistics agency said industrial output in November was 1.8% higher than in October, and 3% higher than the year-earlier month. This was a stronger outcome than expected, with 26 economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal last week estimating that output rose 1.4% on the month earlier.

Figures for October were revised higher, and Eurostat now estimates that output fell 0.8% during the month, having previously calculated they fell 1.1%.

The rise in output compared with the month earlier was the largest since May 2010, when output jumped 2%. When compared with the year-earlier period, the increase was the largest since August 2011, when output surged 5.5%.

The increase in industrial production was spread across much of the euro zone, with Germany recording a 2.4% rise, France a 1.4% increase, Spain a 1% rise and Italy a more modest 0.3% increase. Output also rose in Ireland and Portugal, but fell 2.2% in Greece. Estonia was the only other member to record a decline.

The rise was also spread across a number of different industries, led by manufacturers of capital goods, and including makers of intermediate and nondurable consumer goods. Manufacture of durable consumer goods fell 0.8%, however, an indication that households haven't yet become confident enough about their prospects to make large purchases, such as of household appliances and cars.

Latvia became the 18th member of the euro zone at the beginning of this year.

SNB Vice President Jean-Pierre Danthine last month said conditions in the Swiss mortgage market have deteriorated in recent months, and this poses the greatest challenge to the stability of the domestically active banks in the medium term.

The worry is that rising residential property prices alongside strong investment buying and falling demand from first-home buyers may eventually pull the rug from under the market, cause a crash and derail Australia's nascent recovery.

"Affordability is the problem," said Paul Brennan, chief economist at Citigroup in Australia, based in Sydney. "Mortgage sizes are rising consistent with the rise in prices and it is getting to a point now where first-home buyers can't get their loan deposit."

Sydney, the country's largest property market, jumped close to 15%, while property prices in Melbourne and Perth also made strong advances. English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LONDON—U.K. factory output was weaker than expected and construction output fell in November, official data showed Friday, suggesting Britain's economic recovery may have slowed in the final quarter of the year.

The Office for National Statistics said factory output was flat between October and November as weaker production of cars and drugs offset higher output of steel, plastics and food products.

Overall industrial output was also flat between October and November, held back by a fall in production of propane, butane and similar products from the North Sea, the ONS said.

Economists polled by The Wall Street Journal had predicted British manufacturing output would rise 0.4%, lifting overall industrial production 0.5%.

Britain's construction sector also reported a poor month, with output falling 4% between October and November, dragged down by a fall in both new building and maintenance.

The slowdown at Britain's factories and the decline in construction follows poor Christmas trading reported by several large retailers this week and data showing Britain's trade deficit with the rest of the world remains stubbornly wide.

The figures may spark concern among Britain's policy makers that the nation's economic recovery is far from assured.

The data hurt sterling, pushing the pound as low as $1.6403 Friday.

"These monthly figures are disappointing. It is a timely reminder that the recovery is not yet secure," said David Kern, chief economist at the British Chambers of Commerce, a business group.

The U.K. economy expanded between 0.8% between the second and third quarters of 2013—an annualized rate of 3.2%. A preliminary estimate of fourth-quarter growth will be published by the ONS on Jan. 28.

Economists said Britain's services sector, which accounts for roughly three-quarters of economic output, will need to put in a strong performance in the final months of 2013 if the recent pace of expansion is to be maintained.

Howard Archer, chief U.K. and European economist at IHS Global Insight, on Friday cut his estimate for fourth-quarter growth in the U.K. to 0.7% from 0.8%.

Friday's data will reinforce Bank of England officials' determination to keep borrowing costs for businesses and households low to nurture recovery, economists said.

BOE rate-setters on Thursday left the central bank's benchmark interest rate at a low of 0.5% and its bond-buying program untouched, maintaining their support for the economy.

Europe
Industry Boosts Euro Zone; But British Factory Output Surprisingly Flat
Paul Hannon
658 words
10 January 2014
05:09
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Industrial output in three of the euro zone's largest members rebounded strongly in November, according to official data, indicating that the currency area's economy was on track for a third straight quarter of growth as 2013 drew to a close.

Figures also released Friday showed British factories didn't share in that pickup, with output surprisingly flat during the month, an indication that the U.K.'s recent growth advantage over its eurozone counterparts may be narrowing.

While business surveys have indicated that the euro-zone economy continued to expand in the fourth quarter, official figures had raised some doubts. In particular, figures for October recorded a drop in industrial production, construction output and retail sales.

The European Union's official statistics agency will release figures for industrial production in the euro zone during November on Jan. 14. Releases Thursday and Friday from national agencies in Germany, France, and Spain suggest they will record a pickup.

The revival in industry will help reassure policy makers that the still-fragile recovery was sustained through the final months of last year. European Central Bank President Mario Draghi Thursday said that while the economy continued to show signs of "broad-based weakness," it was nonetheless on the mend.

"While developments in industrial production data for October point to a weak start to the fourth quarter, survey-based confidence indicators up to December have improved further from low levels, overall indicating a continuation of the gradual recovery in economic activity," he said.

French statistics agency Insee said Friday industrial production in the euro zone's second-largest economy rose 1.3% in November from the previous month, while Spanish agency INE said industrial production in the currency area's fourth-largest economy rose by 1.1%.

That followed a release from Germany's economics ministry Thursday that recorded a 1.9% jump in output.

Violante di Canossa, an economist at Credit Suisse, said those figures suggest the euro-zone economy grew at an annualized rate of 1% in the fourth quarter, a pickup from the 0.4% rate of growth recorded in the third quarter.

While a member of the EU, Sweden has chosen to remain outside the euro zone. Data released Friday showed it recorded an even larger rise in industrial production during November, a 5.7% rise from October.

Economists said that partly reflected the reopening of a large oil refinery in southern Sweden, but suggests the economy may be picking up after a period of weakness that included a 0.1% expansion in the third quarter following a 0.1% contraction in the second.

"We see today's outcome as a sign of a brewing recovery in the manufacturing and service sector, and confirm our forecast of 0.5%…growth…in 4Q GDP," said Tina Mortensen, an economist at Citigroup.

By contrast, figures released by the U.K.'s Office for National Statistics showed factory output was flat between October and November, while construction output fell 4% between October and November. Ms. di Canossa said that suggests growth in the fourth quarter "could be comparatively soft."

The U.K. economy grew by 0.8% in the third quarter, equivalent to an annualized rate of 3.2%. The National Institute for Economic Research—an independent research body—Friday estimated the economy grew by 0.7% in the three months through December. NIESR's estimates have proved accurate in the past.

Also Friday, the Bank of France said its monthly business survey indicates the French economy will rebound in the last quarter of 2013 after a contraction in the third quarter as predicted previously.

The December business survey showed the French gross domestic product will grow 0.5% in the fourth quarter from the third, when it contracted 0.1%. The forecast is in unchanged from the Bank of France's previous estimates for the quarter.

Asia
Housing Data Bode Well for Australia's Economy; New-Home Sales Rise at Fastest Pace in Almost Four Years, Bolstering Hopes of a Broader Recovery
James Glynn
413 words
9 January 2014
19:56
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

SYDNEY—For Australian policy makers looking for evidence of a sustainable economic recovery, the latest data on sales of newly built homes will come as a relief.

A government report Friday showed new-home sales rose at the fastest pace in almost four years in November, boding well for a broader recovery dependent on a pickup in areas such as construction and high-street spending.

After all, the more new homes that are sold, the greater the need for occupants to buy furniture, carpets and other household goods. Also, the more solid the demand for new houses and apartments, the more work there is for the nation's builders.

It is exactly the virtuous circle policy makers in the resource-rich nation are hoping to create to offset a slowing mining boom after more than a year of lackluster economic performance.

The central bank has cut interest rates eight times in the past two years to a record-low 2.5% to help other sectors take up the slack from the resource industry, which lost thousands of jobs as China's economy slowed and major mining projects reached or neared completion.

Friday's data from the Housing Industry Association, an umbrella group for the nation's major home builders, showed new home sales jumped 7.5% in November from October. It was the biggest monthly increase since January 2010, and the latest indication that the low interest rates are in fact fueling a recovery in the nonmining economy.

"We need to see upward momentum in these leading indicators continue throughout 2014," said Harley Dale, HIA's chief economist.

The housing market overall had runaway success last year, with prices nationally rising at the fastest pace in more than three years. The sharp rises have created some of the most expensive property in the world, but have fanned concern the market may be overheating and that a sudden crash might undo a still-fragile economic recovery.

Still, recent data have helped ease worries that the house-price gains are wildly disproportionate to improvements elsewhere in the Australian economy.

Aside from the latest new-home-sales figures, a government report Thursday showed retail sales climbed for the seventh month in a row in November, while measures of business and consumer confidence also showed significant gains last year.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.
